Severity Order and the Ruling on Ta'zir Being Most Severe
Ruling on severity order and distribution debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of severity of beating punishments and explain why ta'zir is considered the most severe despite having fewer lashes.

Core Idea
Ta'zir is most severe in intensity even though lenient in number – severity is in each stroke, not the count.

The Hanafi school ranks the severity of beating in Islamic punishments: ta'zir (discretionary) is the most severe, followed by the fixed hadd for zina, then for drinking alcohol, then for false accusation (qadhf). This may seem counterintuitive because ta'zir has fewer lashes, but the severity refers to the intensity of each blow. The passage also discusses a key intra-Hanafi debate: should ta'zir strokes be concentrated on one spot or distributed over the body? Understanding this order is essential for a judge or jurist when assigning punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
From the text, extract the four punishments in order of severity: ta'zir, zina hadd, shurb hadd, qadhf hadd.
2
Understand the Reasoning for Ta'zir's Severity
The text states that leniency in number (fewer lashes) is compensated by intensity in description (stronger blows). Explain why: to achieve the purpose of deterrence without exceeding prescribed limits.
This is a unique Hanafi perspective.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Debate on Stroke Distribution
Some Hanafi authorities hold that when ta'zir is at its maximum (39 lashes), strokes should be concentrated on one spot; others say intensity should come from the force of the blow, not concentration. Identify which view is preferred in the text.
The preferred view is to concentrate at maximum severity.
4
Recognize the Place of Avoidance in Striking
The text notes that the same places avoided in hadd are avoided in ta'zir (vital organs). List these: back and buttocks only.
5
Connect to Wider Fiqh
Realise that this order impacts judicial practice: when multiple punishments combine, ta'zir is executed first because it is purely a right of the servant, while hudud are rights of Allah.
This prioritization is mentioned at the end of the selected passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحدود/ باب حد القذف
وأشد الضرب التعزير ثم حد الزنا ثم الشرب ثم القذف ومن حد أو عزر فمات فدمه هدر بخلاف الزوج إذا عزر زوجته لترك الزينة والإجابة إذا دعاها إلى فراشه وترك الصلاة والخروج من البيت. وأشد الضرب التعزير لأنه جرى التخفيف فيه من حيث العدد فلا يخفف من حيث الوصف كيلا يؤدي إلى فوات المقصود. ولم يذكر المصنف أنه يفرق على الأعضاء كضرب الحدود لأنه لا يفرق كما في الهداية وإليه يشير إطلاق الأشدية الشاملة لقوته وجمعه في عضو واحد، وفي حدود الأصل يفرق التعزير على الأعضاء وفي أشربة الأصل يضرب التعزير في موضع واحد قال في التبيين وليس في المسألة اختلاف الرواية وإنما اختلف الجواب لاختلاف الموضوع، فموضوع الأول إذا بلغ بالتعزير أقصاه، وموضوع الثاني إذا لم يبلغ ا هـ . وهكذا في المجتبى وفي فتح القدير، وأثبت الاختلاف في غاية البيان معزياً إلى الأسبيجابي فقال بعضهم : الشدة هو الجمع فتجمع الأسواط في عضو واحد ولا يفرق على الأعضاء بخلاف سائر الحدود. وقال بعضهم : لا بل شدته في الضرب لا في الجمع اهـ. قالوا: ويتقي المواضع التي تتقى في الحدود. قال في المجتبى : ويضرب الظهر والألية. قالوا : ويبلغ في التعزير غايته وهو تسعة وثلاثون سوطاً فيما إذا أصاب من الأجنبية كل محرم غير الجماع، وفيما إذا أخذ السارق بعد ما جمع المتاع قبل الإخراج، وفيما إذا شتمه بجنس يجب به حد القذف كقوله للعبد أو الذمي يا زاني. وأشار بالأشدية إلى أنه يجرد من ثيابه. قال في غاية البيان ويجرد في سائر الحدود إلا في حد القذف فإنه يضرب وعليه ثيابه كما قدمناه. ويخالفه ما في فتاوى قاضيخان يضرب للتعزير قائماً عليه ثيابه وينزع الفرو والحشو ولا يمد في التعزير .اهـ. والظاهر الأول لتصريح المبسوط به وإلى أنه لو اجتمع التعزير مع الحدود قدم التعزير في الاستيفاء لتمحضه حقاً للعبد ؛ كذا في الظهيرية قوله : (ثم حد الزنا) لأنه ثابت بالكتاب وحد الشرب ثابت بقول الصحابة رضي الله عنهم ولأنه أعظم جناية حتى شرع فيه الرجم قوله : (ثم الشرب ثم القذف) يعني حد الشرب يلي حد الزنا في شدة الضرب لما قدمناه وحد القذف أدنى الكل وإن كان ثابتاً بالكتاب إلا أن سببه محتمل لاحتمال كونه صادقاً وسبب حد الشرب متيقن به وهو الشرب والمراد أن الشرب متيقن السببية للحد لا متيقن الثبوت لأنه بالبينة أو الإقرار وهما لا يوجبان اليقين قوله : (ومن حد أو عزر فمات فدمه هدر لأنه فعل ما فعل بأمر الشارع وفعل المأمور لا يتقيد بشرط السلامة كالفصاد والبزاغ . قال في ضياء الحلوم : ذهب دمه هدراً أي باطلاً.
The most severe beating is ta'zir, then the hadd of zina, then [the hadd of] shurb, then [the hadd of] qadhf. And whoever is subjected to hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is worthless, unlike the husband when he gives ta'zir to his wife for abandoning adornment and responding when he calls her to his bed, and for abandoning prayer and leaving the house. And the most severe beating is ta'zir because leniency was applied in it regarding number, so it is not lenient in description so as not to lead to loss of purpose. The author did not mention that it is distributed over the limbs like the beating of hadd because it is not distributed, as in al-Hidayah, and his absolute wording of 'most severe' indicates its strength and concentration in one limb. In the limits of al-Asl, ta'zir is distributed over the limbs, but in the drinks of al-Asl, ta'zir is struck in one place. Al-Tabayini said: There is no difference of narration in the issue; rather the answer differs due to different contexts. The first context is when ta'zir reaches its maximum, the second when it does not. Similarly in al-Mujtaba and Fath al-Qadir. And he affirmed the difference in Ghayat al-Bayan, attributing to al-Isbijabi: Some said: Severity is concentration, so the lashes are gathered in one limb and not distributed, unlike other hadds. Some said: No, rather severity is in the beating itself, not in concentration. They said: The places that are avoided in hadd are avoided [here]. Al-Mujtaba said: The back and buttocks are struck. They said: Ta'zir reaches its maximum of thirty-nine lashes in case of touching a non-mahram woman without intercourse, or a thief after gathering goods before removal, or if he insults him with something that would require hadd qadhf, like saying to a slave or a dhimmi 'O fornicator.' And by 'most severe' he indicated that he is stripped of his clothes. Ghayat al-Bayan: He is stripped in all hudud except hadd qadhf, where he is struck with his clothes on, as we mentioned. Contrary to Fatawa Qadikhan: He is struck standing with his clothes on, removing fur and stuffing, and he is not stretched in ta'zir. The first is apparent due to the expression of al-Mabsut. And if ta'zir combines with hudud, ta'zir is prioritized in execution because it is purely a right of the slave. Thus in al-Dhahiriyya. Then he said: Then hadd zina because it is established by the Book, and hadd shurb is established by the statement of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and because it is a greater crime, stoning was prescribed in it. Then he said: Then shurb then qadhf, meaning hadd shurb follows hadd zina in severity of beating as we mentioned, and hadd qadhf is the least of all, even though it is established by the Book, but its cause is probable because he may be truthful, while the cause of hadd shurb is certain—shurb. The intent is that shurb is certain in causality for the hadd, not certain in establishment, because it is by evidence or confession, and both do not necessitate certainty. Then he said: Whoever is subjected to hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is worthless because he did what he did by command of the Lawgiver, and the act of the commanded is not conditioned on safety, like phlebotomy and cupping. Al-Dhiya' al-Hulw said: His blood goes waste, i.e., null.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage covers two interconnected rulings. First, it establishes the order of severity of beating in Islamic punishments: ta'zir (discretionary) is the most severe, followed by the prescribed hadd for zina (adultery/fornication), then for drinking alcohol (shurb), and finally for false accusation (qadhf). The author explains that ta'zir is most severe in description (intensity) despite having fewer lashes, to achieve its purpose. There is discussion among Hanafi scholars whether ta'zir strokes should be concentrated in one spot or distributed. The relied-upon position is that when ta'zir is at its maximum (39 lashes), concentration is used; otherwise, distribution is allowed. Second, the passage deals with the rule that if a person dies due to a lawfully administered hadd or ta'zir, there is no liability—the blood is 'wasted' (haddar). The justification is that the executioner acts under divine command, similar to phlebotomy. An important exception is mentioned: if a husband beats his wife for specific reasons (abandoning adornment, refusing to come to bed, neglecting prayer, leaving home without permission) and she dies, he is liable because his authority is permissive, not commanded. This distinction between commanding (amr) and permitting (idhn) is fundamental in Hanafi law for determining liability.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zīr): A discretionary punishment set by the judge for offenses without fixed hadd.
الحد (al-ḥadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by the Lawgiver for specific offenses.
هدر (hadar): Wasted; no liability for blood money or retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is ta'zir considered more severe (أشد) than other hudud in terms of beating, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because it has the highest number of lashes
B) Because the strokes are more intense and concentrated at maximum
C) Because it is established by the Quran, unlike other hudud
D) Because the criminal is stripped naked during execution

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ta'zir is the most severe beating punishment in terms of intensity, even though its maximum number of lashes (39) is less than some hudud.

Death During Lawful Punishment: No Liability and the Husband Exception
Ruling on liability for death from lawful punishment and exception for husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that death from lawfully administered hadd or ta'zir incurs no liability, and distinguish the exception for a husband's ta'zir based on the distinction between command and permission.

Core Idea
Blood is wasted if death occurs from a commanded punishment, but the husband acts under permission, not command, so he is liable.

The passage addresses a critical principle in the law of punishments: what happens if the offender dies while undergoing a lawful punishment? The Hanafi school rules that no liability (no diya, no qisas) arises because the executioner is acting under a command from the Lawgiver. However, there is a significant exception: if a husband disciplines his wife and she dies, he is liable because his authority is permissive, not commanded. This distinction between 'amr' (command) and 'idhn' (permission) is fundamental in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
From the text, identify the ruling: 'Whoever is subjected to hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is worthless (haddar).' This means no diya or qisas.
2
Examine the Rationale (Talil)
The text explains: the executor acted by the command of the Lawgiver. Acts performed under command are not conditioned on safety – analogy to phlebotomy and cupping, where death is also not penalized.
This is a strong analogy (qiyas) from medical procedures.
3
Identify the Exception
The exception is the husband when he gives ta'zir to his wife for specific reasons (abandoning adornment, refusing to come to bed, neglecting prayer, leaving home without permission). If she dies, he is liable.
Why? Because his authority is derived from permission (shar'an), not command. The distinction is between wajib/punitive and mubah/discretionary.
4
Understand the Legal Principle Behind the Exception
The husband's ta'zir is based on the verse 'and beat them' (Quran 4:34) which is interpreted as permissive, not obligatory. Thus, it is not a commanded act like the judge's execution of hadd. Therefore, liability attaches.
Some may argue the verse is a command, but the Hanafi school interprets it as permission in this context.
5
Apply to Modern Scenarios
This ruling is still relevant in Islamic jurisdictions that implement hudud and ta'zir. The state executioner is immune from liability, but a husband cannot use excessive force without consequences.
The modern mapping also applies to any state-authorized corporal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The most severe beating is ta'zir, then the hadd of zina, then [the hadd of] shurb, then [the hadd of] qadhf. And whoever is subjected to hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is worthless, unlike the husband when he gives ta'zir to his wife for abandoning adornment and responding when he calls her to his bed, and for abandoning prayer and leaving the house. And the most severe beating is ta'zir because leniency was applied in it regarding number, so it is not lenient in description so as not to lead to loss of purpose. The author did not mention that it is distributed over the limbs like the beating of hadd because it is not distributed, as in al-Hidayah, and his absolute wording of 'most severe' indicates its strength and concentration in one limb. In the limits of al-Asl, ta'zir is distributed over the limbs, but in the drinks of al-Asl, ta'zir is struck in one place. Al-Tabayini said: There is no difference of narration in the issue; rather the answer differs due to different contexts. The first context is when ta'zir reaches its maximum, the second when it does not. Similarly in al-Mujtaba and Fath al-Qadir. And he affirmed the difference in Ghayat al-Bayan, attributing to al-Isbijabi: Some said: Severity is concentration, so the lashes are gathered in one limb and not distributed, unlike other hadds. Some said: No, rather severity is in the beating itself, not in concentration. They said: The places that are avoided in hadd are avoided [here]. Al-Mujtaba said: The back and buttocks are struck. They said: Ta'zir reaches its maximum of thirty-nine lashes in case of touching a non-mahram woman without intercourse, or a thief after gathering goods before removal, or if he insults him with something that would require hadd qadhf, like saying to a slave or a dhimmi 'O fornicator.' And by 'most severe' he indicated that he is stripped of his clothes. Ghayat al-Bayan: He is stripped in all hudud except hadd qadhf, where he is struck with his clothes on, as we mentioned. Contrary to Fatawa Qadikhan: He is struck standing with his clothes on, removing fur and stuffing, and he is not stretched in ta'zir. The first is apparent due to the expression of al-Mabsut. And if ta'zir combines with hudud, ta'zir is prioritized in execution because it is purely a right of the slave. Thus in al-Dhahiriyya. Then he said: Then hadd zina because it is established by the Book, and hadd shurb is established by the statement of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and because it is a greater crime, stoning was prescribed in it. Then he said: Then shurb then qadhf, meaning hadd shurb follows hadd zina in severity of beating as we mentioned, and hadd qadhf is the least of all, even though it is established by the Book, but its cause is probable because he may be truthful, while the cause of hadd shurb is certain—shurb. The intent is that shurb is certain in causality for the hadd, not certain in establishment, because it is by evidence or confession, and both do not necessitate certainty. Then he said: Whoever is subjected to hadd or ta'zir and dies, his blood is worthless because he did what he did by command of the Lawgiver, and the act of the commanded is not conditioned on safety, like phlebotomy and cupping. Al-Dhiya' al-Hulw said: His blood goes waste, i.e., null.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage covers two interconnected rulings. First, it establishes the order of severity of beating in Islamic punishments: ta'zir (discretionary) is the most severe, followed by the prescribed hadd for zina (adultery/fornication), then for drinking alcohol (shurb), and finally for false accusation (qadhf). The author explains that ta'zir is most severe in description (intensity) despite having fewer lashes, to achieve its purpose. There is discussion among Hanafi scholars whether ta'zir strokes should be concentrated in one spot or distributed. The relied-upon position is that when ta'zir is at its maximum (39 lashes), concentration is used; otherwise, distribution is allowed. Second, the passage deals with the rule that if a person dies due to a lawfully administered hadd or ta'zir, there is no liability—the blood is 'wasted' (haddar). The justification is that the executioner acts under divine command, similar to phlebotomy. An important exception is mentioned: if a husband beats his wife for specific reasons (abandoning adornment, refusing to come to bed, neglecting prayer, leaving home without permission) and she dies, he is liable because his authority is permissive, not commanded. This distinction between commanding (amr) and permitting (idhn) is fundamental in Hanafi law for determining liability.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Wasted; no liability for blood money or retaliation.
أمر الشارع (amr al-shāri'): Command of the Lawgiver; a directive that is binding and must be executed.
إذن (idhn): Permission; an act that is allowed but not commanded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the husband's ta'zir treated differently from state-administered ta'zir when death occurs?
A) Because the state uses a whip, while the husband uses his hand
B) Because the husband acts under permission, not command
C) Because the wife is a woman, and different rules apply to women
D) Because the husband's ta'zir is not a real punishment

Key Takeaway
The core principle: death from a commanded punishment incurs no liability; the husband's case is an exception because his authority is only permissive.

Liability for Death: Commanded vs Permitted Acts
Core ruling on liability distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a commanded act (mamur bihi) and a permitted act (madhun fihi) and apply the Hanafi rule that commanded acts waive liability for death while permitted acts retain it.

Core Idea
Commanded acts waive liability; permitted acts require safety – husband's discipline is permitted, so he is liable if she dies.

This lesson examines the foundational principle that the husband's authority to discipline his wife is permissive, not commanded. Unlike state-administered punishments, if the wife dies from the beating, the husband must pay compensation. This distinction between 'amr' and 'idhn' is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for determining liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognise the terms 'mamur bihi' (commanded) and 'madhun fihi' (permitted) as they appear in the text. The text states that every beating commanded by the Lawgiver removes liability, while every beating merely permitted retains liability.
The key is whether the act is obligatory (wajib) or simply permissible.
2
Understand the legal consequence
For a commanded act, the doer is not liable for unintended death because the act is required. For a permitted act, the doer is liable because the permission is conditional on safety.
The example given: walking on the road (permitted) – if death occurs, liability exists.
3
Apply to the husband's ta'zir
The husband's beating of his wife is permitted, not commanded. Therefore, if she dies, he is liable (daman). The text explicitly states that her blood is not wasted (hader).
This contrasts with state-administered ta'zir, which is commanded and thus exempts the executor.
4
Note the four permissible grounds
The text lists four specific situations where beating is permitted: abandoning adornment, refusing to come to bed, neglecting prayer, and leaving the house without right. These are the only explicit grounds, but the principle extends to similar matters (as seen in later lessons).
Beating is not obligatory (wajib) for any ground; it is merely permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بخلاف الزوج إذا عزر زوجته لترك الزينة والإجابة إذا دعاها إلى فراشه وترك الصلاة والخروج من البيت. الصلاة والخروج من البيت يعني فماتت فإنه يكون ضامناً ولا يكون دمها هدراً لأنه مباح ومنفعته ترجع إليه كما ترجع إلى المرأة من وجه وهو استقامتها على ما أمر الله تعالى به. وقد ظهر بهذا أن كل ضرب كان مأموراً به من جهة الشارع فإن الضارب لا ضمان عليه بموته، وكل ضرب كان مأذوناً فيه بدون الأمر فإن الضارب يضمنه إذا مات لتقييده بشرط السلامة كالمرور في الطريق. وظهر أن الزوج لا يجب عليه ضرب زوجته أصلاً، وظهر به أيضاً أن له ضربها في أربعة مواضع لكن وقع الاختلاف في جواز ضربها على ترك الصلاة فذكر هنا تبعاً لكثير أنه يجوز ، وفي النهاية تبعاً لما في كافي الحاكم أنه لا يجوز له لأن المنفعة لا تعود إليه بل إليها، وليس في كلام المصنف ما يقتضي أنه ليس له ضربها في غير هذه أربعة الأشياء ولهذا قال الولوالجي في فتاواه للزوج أن يضرب زوجته على أربعة أشياء وما في معناها. ففي قوله «وما في معناها إفادة عدم الحصر فمما في معناها ما إذا ضربت جارية زوجها غيرة ولا تتعظ بوعظه فله ضربها ؛ كذا في القنية. وينبغي أن يلحق به ما إذا ضربت الولد الذي لا يعقل عند بكائه لأن ضرب الدابة إذا كان ممنوعاً فهذا أولى. ومنه ما إذا شتمته أو مزقت ثيابه أو أخذت لحيته أو قالت له يا حمار يا أبله أو لعنته، سواء شتمها أو لا على قول العامة. ومنه ما إذا شتمت أجنبياً، ومنه ما إذا كشفت وجهها لغير محرم أو كلمت أجنبياً أو تكلمت عامداً مع الزوج أو شاغبت معه ليسمع صوتها الأجنبي. . ومنه ما إذا أعطت من بيته شيئاً من الطعام بلا إذنه حيث كانت العادة لم تجر به وإن كانت العادة مسامحة المرأة بذلك بلا مشورة الزوج فليس له ضربها. ومنه ما إذا ادعت عليه وليس منه ما إذا طلبت نفقتها أو كسوتها وألحت لأن لصاحب الحق يد الملازمة ولسان التقاضي ؛ كذا أفاده في البزازية في مسائل الضرب من فصل الأمر باليد والمعنى الجامع للكل أنها إذا ارتكبت معصية ليس فيها حد مقدر فإن للزوج أن يعزرها كما أن للسيد ذلك بعبده ؛ كذا في البدائع من فصل القسم بين شامل لما كان متعلقاً بالزوج وبغيره. وقد صرحوا بأنه إذا ضربها بغير حق وجب عليه التعزير، ولا يخفى أنه إنما يجوز ضربها لترك الزينة إذا كانت قادرة عليها وكانت شرعية وإلا فلا كما أنه يجوز ضربها لترك الإجابة إذا كانت طاهرة عن الحيض وعن النفاس، وكما يجوز ضربها للخروج إذا كان الخروج بغير حق، وأما إذا كان بحق فليس له ضربها عليه، وقدمنا النساء وهو في الشرح وهو صريح في دفع التردد السابق. قوله : (أو قالت له يا حمار يا أبله) قال في النهر : ينبغي في ظاهر الرواية عدم التعزير فيهما، وعلى القول الثاني إن كان المقول له من الأشراف أن يعزر القائل وإلا لا ينبغي أن يفعل في الزوج إلا أن يفرق بين الزوجة وغيرها والموضع يحتاج إلى تدبر وتأمل.
His saying: (Unlike the husband when he gives ta'zir to his wife for abandoning adornment and responding when he calls her to his bed, and for abandoning prayer and leaving the house. Prayer and leaving the house) means that if she dies, he is liable and her blood is not wasted because it is permissible and its benefit returns to him as it returns to the woman in some respect, namely her uprightness according to what Allah commanded. From this it appears that every beating that is commanded by the Lawgiver, the beater is not liable for death; but every beating that is merely permitted without command, the beater is liable if death occurs because it is conditioned on safety, like walking on the road. And it appears that the husband is not obligated to beat his wife at all; and it also appears that he may beat her in four situations, but there is disagreement on beating her for abandoning prayer. Here he follows many in deeming it permissible, but in al-Nihaya he follows al-Hakim in that he may not, because the benefit does not return to him but to her. And the author's words do not imply that he cannot beat her for other than these four things; hence al-Walwaliji said in his Fatawa: The husband may beat his wife for four things and similar matters. By 'similar matters' he indicates that it is not limited. So from similar matters: if she strikes his slave girl out of jealousy and is not deterred by advice, he may beat her; as in al-Qunyah. And it is appropriate to add the case where she strikes a child who has no understanding when he cries, because beating an animal is prohibited, so this is more deserving. Also if she insults him, tears his clothes, grabs his beard, or calls him 'O donkey' or 'O fool', or curses him, whether she insults him or not, according to the general opinion. Also if she insults a stranger, or if she uncovers her face before a non-mahram, or speaks to a stranger, or intentionally speaks loudly with her husband so that a stranger hears her voice. Also if she gives away food from his house without permission when custom does not permit it; if custom permits her to do so without consulting her husband, then he may not beat her. Also if she makes a claim against him, but not if she demands her maintenance or clothing persistently, because the right holder has the right of persistence and the tongue of litigation. As in al-Bazzaziyya in the section on beating. The common denominator is that if she commits a sin that has no fixed hadd, the husband may discipline her (ta'zir), just as the master may discipline his slave. As in al-Bada'i'. And they explicitly say that if he beats her without right, he must be subjected to ta'zir. It is clear that beating for abandoning adornment is only permissible if she is capable of it and it is lawful adornment; otherwise not. Similarly, beating for refusing to answer is permissible only if she is pure from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And beating for leaving the house is permissible only if the leaving is without right; if it is with right, he may not beat her. We have already mentioned women, and it is in the commentary, and it is explicit in removing the previous hesitation. His saying: (Or she says to him 'O donkey' or 'O fool') said in al-Nahr: In the apparent narration, there is no ta'zir for either. On the second opinion, if the addressee is of noble status, the speaker is disciplined; otherwise it is not done, except the case of the husband, but a distinction may be made between the wife and others; the matter requires reflection and deliberation.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage expands on the exception to the death rule: while state-administered punishments causing death carry no liability, a husband who beats his wife causing her death is liable. The reason is that the husband's authority is permissive (idhn) rather than commanded (amr). The husband may beat his wife in four specific cases: (1) abandoning adornment, (2) refusing to come to bed, (3) neglecting prayer, (4) leaving home without permission. There is disagreement on beating for prayer: some Hanafi scholars permit it, others do not because the benefit (uprightness in religion) accrues to the wife alone. The relied-upon position is permissibility. The passage then lists analogous situations: striking the husband's slave girl, hitting a crying child, insulting the husband, damaging property, speaking to strangers, etc. The common principle is that for any sin without a fixed hadd, the husband may administer ta'zir. However, beating for demanding lawful rights (like maintenance) is not allowed. If the husband beats without justification, he himself is subject to ta'zir. The passage clarifies that the four mentioned acts are not exhaustive; any act 'in their meaning' permits discipline. The final note addresses the case of a wife calling her husband 'donkey' or 'fool', where the Hanafi position is that no ta'zir is due in the apparent narration, but if the husband is a noble, ta'zir may apply.

Key Terms
مأمور به (ma'mūr bi-hi): An act commanded by the Lawgiver, removing liability for unintended consequences.
مأذون فيه (ma'dhūn fī-hi): An act permitted but not commanded; conditions of safety apply.
التعزير (al-ta'zīr): Discretionary punishment; here, the husband's discipline of his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a husband beats his wife for abandoning adornment and she dies?
A) The husband is not liable because beating for adornment is commanded.
B) The husband is liable because his authority is permissive.
C) The husband is not liable because the wife was disobedient.
D) The husband is only liable if he beat her excessively.

Key Takeaway
Only commanded acts remove liability for death; permitted acts like husband's discipline carry liability.

The Four Grounds and the Prayer Dispute
Expansion on the four grounds and the disputed prayer ground

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four grounds for husband's discipline, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on beating for neglecting prayer, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Husband may beat for adornment, bed, prayer, and leaving home – but the prayer ground is disputed among Hanafis.

This lesson focuses on the four specific situations in which a husband may discipline his wife. While three grounds are widely accepted, the permissibility of beating for neglecting prayer is contested: some Hanafis permit it (benefit to husband in her uprightness), while others prohibit it (benefit solely to wife). The relied-upon position allows it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four grounds
From the passage, list: (1) abandoning adornment (ترك الزينة), (2) refusing to come to bed (امتناع عن الإجابة), (3) neglecting prayer (ترك الصلاة), (4) leaving the house without right (الخروج بغير حق).
These are the explicit grounds mentioned in the text.
2
Understand the disagreement on prayer
The text notes a difference among Hanafis: many scholars permit beating for prayer (the author's view here), while al-Hakim in al-Kafi prohibits it because the benefit (uprightness in faith) accrues to the wife alone.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not between schools.
3
Analyse the opposing proofs
Pro-permissibility: the husband benefits indirectly from his wife's uprightness in marital life. Pro-prohibition: the benefit is purely religious, not a right of the husband.
The text favours permissibility (تباعاً لكثير).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The author's reliance on many scholars and al-Walwaliji's fatwa indicate that the operative position is permissibility, with liability for death.
This is the view used in fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بخلاف الزوج إذا عزر زوجته لترك الزينة والإجابة إذا دعاها إلى فراشه وترك الصلاة والخروج من البيت. الصلاة والخروج من البيت يعني فماتت فإنه يكون ضامناً ولا يكون دمها هدراً لأنه مباح ومنفعته ترجع أفاده في البزازية في مسائل الضرب من فصل الأمر باليد والمعنى الجامع للكل أنها إذا ارتكبت معصية ليس فيها حد مقدر فإن للزوج أن يعزرها كما أن للسيد ذلك بعبده ؛ كذا في البدائع من فصل القسم بين شامل لما كان متعلقاً بالزوج وبغيره. وقد صرحوا بأنه إذا ضربها بغير حق وجب عليه التعزير، ولا يخفى أنه إنما يجوز ضربها لترك الزينة إذا كانت قادرة عليها وكانت شرعية وإلا فلا كما أنه يجوز ضربها لترك الإجابة إذا كانت إلى تدبر وتأمل.
His saying: (Unlike the husband when he gives ta'zir to his wife for abandoning adornment and responding when he calls her to his bed, and for abandoning prayer and leaving the house. Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage expands on the exception to the death rule: while state-administered punishments causing death carry no liability, a husband who beats his wife causing her death is liable. The reason is that the husband's authority is permissive (idhn) rather than commanded (amr). The husband may beat his wife in four specific cases: (1) abandoning adornment, (2) refusing to come to bed, (3) neglecting prayer, (4) leaving home without permission. There is disagreement on beating for prayer: some Hanafi scholars permit it, others do not because the benefit (uprightness in religion) accrues to the wife alone. The relied-upon position is permissibility. The passage then lists analogous situations: striking the husband's slave girl, hitting a crying child, insulting the husband, damaging property, speaking to strangers, etc. The common principle is that for any sin without a fixed hadd, the husband may administer ta'zir. However, beating for demanding lawful rights (like maintenance) is not allowed. If the husband beats without justification, he himself is subject to ta'zir. The passage clarifies that the four mentioned acts are not exhaustive; any act 'in their meaning' permits discipline. The final note addresses the case of a wife calling her husband 'donkey' or 'fool', where the Hanafi position is that no ta'zir is due in the apparent narration, but if the husband is a noble, ta'zir may apply.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zīr): Discretionary punishment; here, the husband's discipline of his wife.
مأذون فيه (ma'dhūn fī-hi): An act permitted but not commanded; conditions of safety apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The prayer ground is disputed within the Hanafi school, but the mufta bihi permits it, with liability for death.

Analogous Cases and Conditions for Valid Ta'zir
Expansion on similar cases and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify additional cases where a husband may discipline his wife based on the principle of 'similar matters' (ma fi ma'naha), and list the conditions that make the beating valid or invalid.

Core Idea
Any sin without fixed hadd permits husband's ta'zir, but not for demanding lawful rights – and beating without right itself requires ta'zir.

The passage extends the husband's authority beyond the four explicit grounds. Scholars like al-Walwaliji and others include acts that are 'in the meaning' of those four, such as insulting the husband, damaging property, or speaking to strangers. The common denominator is any sin without a fixed hadd. However, beating for demanding lawful rights (like maintenance) is not allowed. This lesson explores the examples and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of 'ma fi ma'naha'
Al-Walwaliji states the husband may beat for four things and 'similar matters'. This principle allows analogical extension to any act that shares the same illa (any sin without fixed hadd).
The text then gives several examples.
2
List the extended cases
Examples from the text: striking the husband's slave girl out of jealousy, hitting a crying child, insulting the husband (calling him donkey/fool), tearing his clothes, grabbing his beard, cursing him, insulting strangers, uncovering face before non-mahram, speaking to strangers, speaking loudly so strangers hear, giving away food without permission (when custom disallows it).
Also includes making a claim against the husband, but NOT demanding maintenance or clothing persistently.
3
Identify the common denominator
The text says: 'the common denominator for all is that if she commits a sin without a fixed hadd, the husband may discipline her.' This is the illa.
Thus any sin without hadd permits ta'zir.
4
Recognise the limits
Beating is NOT allowed for demanding lawful rights like maintenance or clothing, because the right-holder has the right to persist (yad al-mulazamah). Also, beating without right subjects the husband to ta'zir.
Custom also matters: if custom permits giving food without permission, no beating.

Ta'zir for Minors, Slaves, and Wives: Conditions and Applications
Expanded discussion of ta'zir rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on ta'zir for minors, slaves, and wives, and explain the conditions that affect its applicability.

Core Idea
Ta'zir applies to minors for discipline, but puberty is required for rights of Allah; it does not automatically drop with puberty or conversion.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the rulings of ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for various categories: minors, slaves, and wives. It addresses key questions: Is ta'zir valid for children? Does puberty matter? Can a husband strike his wife? Does ta'zir fall if the person reaches puberty or converts? The Hanafi school provides nuanced answers based on the nature of the right violated and the authority of the guardian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of ta'zir for minors
The passage states that ta'zir is legislated for children (الصبيان) and gives an example: a near-adult who insults a scholar must receive ta'zir. Note the distinction between rights of Allah and rights of servants.
This shows that ta'zir is not limited to adults.
2
Distinguish the role of puberty
For rights of Allah (e.g., drinking wine, zina, theft), puberty is a condition for ta'zir. For rights of servants, minors can be subject to ta'zir without puberty.
The passage reconciles differing views from al-Sarakhsi and al-Tarjumani.
3
Examine ta'zir for wives and slaves
The husband may strike his wife for discipline (Quran 4:34). For slaves, the master may ta'zir, but there is a dispute: Abu Bakr al-Razi held no ta'zir by master, while the majority allow it. Teachers and fathers are not liable for damages unless excessive.
Restriction to wife indicates that the same does not apply to parent or teacher without special conditions.
4
Determine whether ta'zir drops with puberty or conversion
The passage reports a Shafi'i view that ta'zir drops with puberty, but al-Ramli rejects this, especially for rights of humans. Similarly, ta'zir does not fall if a dhimmi converts to Islam.
This shows ta'zir is generally not voided by subsequent changes in status.
5
Consolidate the conditions for ta'zir
Summarize who can administer ta'zir (authorities, husband, master, teacher), to whom (minors, wives, slaves), and when (for discipline, not for hadd violations by minors regarding rights of Allah).
Key condition: The act must be sinful, and the punishment must be proportionate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المواضع التي تخرج إليها بغير إذنه في كتاب النفقات. وأطلق في الزوجة فشمل الصغيرة ولذا قال في التبيين : إن التعزير مشروع في حق الصبيان وفي القنية : مراهق شتم عالماً فعليه التعزير اهـ. وفي المجتبى معزياً إلى السرخسي : الصغير لا يمنع وجوب التعزير ولو كان حقاً الله تعالى لمنع. وعن الترجماني : البلوغ يعتبر في التعزير أراد به ما حقاً الله تعالى وجب نحو ما إذا شرب الصبي أو زنى أو سرق، وما ذكره السرخسي فيما يجب حقاً للعبد توفيقاً بينهما اهـ. قيد بالزوجة لا بالأب والمعلم لا يضمن وفي القنية: ولا يجوز ضرب أختها الصغيرة التي ليس لها ولي بترك الصلاة إذا بلغت عشراً، وله أن يضرب اليتيم فيما يضرب ولده، به وردت الآثار والأخبار. وفي الروضة : له أن يكره ولده الصغير على تعلم القرآن والأدب والعلم لأن ذلك فرض على الوالدين، ولو أمر غيره بضرب عبده حل للمأمور ضربه بخلاف الحر. قال رضي الله عنه : فهذا تنصيص على عدم جواز ضرب ولد الآمر بأمره بخلاف المعلم لأن المأمور يضربه نيابة عن الأب لمصلحته والمعلم يضربه بحكم الملك بتمليك أبيه لمصلحة الولد .اهـ. وفيها أيضاً عن أبي بكر : أساء عبده لا يعزره وهذا خلاف قول أصحابنا، وله التعزير دون الحد وبه نأخذ وكذلك امرأته لأن الله تعالى قال واضربوهن [النساء : ٣٤] اهـ والله أعلم.

The places where she goes out without his permission in the Book of Nafaqat. And he made it absolute regarding the wife, so it includes the minor, hence it was said in al-Tabyin: Indeed ta'zir is legislated for children. And in al-Qunyah: A near-adult who insults a scholar must be ta'zir. End. And from al-Tarjumani: Puberty is considered in ta'zir, he meant that which is a right of Allah becomes obligatory, such as when a child drinks wine, commits adultery, or steals; and what al-Sarakhsi mentioned regarding what is a right of the servant is a reconciliation between them. End. And Allah knows best.
Accept: And I have not seen the ruling of a child if ta'zir became obligatory upon him for discipline and then he reached puberty, and Fakhr al-Razi transmitted from the Shafi'is that it drops because his rebuke is by puberty. Al-Ramli said here, may Allah have mercy on him: There is no basis for its dropping, especially if it is not a right of Allah but rather a right of a human being. So ponder his statement: (He restricted to the wife, not the father and teacher, thus in some copies. And in some, because the father and teacher are not liable, but in al-Tanwir and its commentary from al-Shumni: If the teacher beats the child severely, he is subjected to ta'zir and guarantees him if he dies.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) from various Hanafi sources. The author, al-Haskafi, in his commentary Al-Bahr al-Raiq, begins by linking to a previous discussion on nafaqah (maintenance) regarding places a wife may go out without permission. He then expands on ta'zir for minors, citing that ta'zir is legislated for children, and a near-adult who insults a scholar must receive ta'zir. Al-Sarakhsi is quoted stating that a minor does not prevent the obligation of ta'zir, but if it were a right of Allah (e.g., punishment for drinking wine), the minor's status would prevent it. Al-Tarjumani holds that puberty is considered in ta'zir, meaning that for rights of Allah (like hadd punishments), puberty is required, but ta'zir for rights of servants may apply to minors. The author reconciles these views. The passage then discusses that a husband may strike his wife for discipline (based on Quran 4:34), but a father or teacher is not liable for corrective discipline unless excessive. It covers rules for orphans, slaves, and the permissibility of compelling children to learn. It also addresses a dispute: Abu Bakr al-Razi held that a master should not ta'zir his slave, but the preferred Hanafi view is that ta'zir is allowed. The passage concludes with a discussion on whether ta'zir drops if the person reaches puberty or converts to Islam, with al-Ramli favoring non-drop for rights of humans.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses not covered by hadd or qisas, determined by the judge or authority.
صبي (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty.
ولي (wali): Guardian; typically the father or legal guardian responsible for a child's upbringing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The minor is still subject to ta'zir.
B) The minor is not subject to ta'zir because puberty is required for rights of Allah.
C) The minor is subject to the hadd punishment instead.
D) The minor must be referred to a judge for a special ruling.

Key Takeaway
Ta'zir for minors is valid for rights of servants but not for rights of Allah unless puberty is reached; it does not automatically drop with puberty or conversion.

Definition of Theft and Conditions for Hand Amputation
Opening of Book of Theft defining theft and its legal conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of theft (sariqa) for the purpose of hadd amputation and identify its five essential conditions.

Core Idea
Amputation for theft requires a legally responsible person, a guarded place, secrecy, and ten dirhams.

This lesson unpacks the legal definition of theft (sariqa) as presented in Kitab al-Sariqa of Al-Bahr al-Raiq. Unlike the everyday understanding, the Hanafi school defines theft for amputation with precise conditions. We will break down each condition — mukallaf, secrecy, nisab, hirz, and the nature of taking — and see how they apply in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of theft
The passage distinguishes between a general prohibition-based definition (taking secretly without right) and a definition for amputation (including specific conditions). Focus on the latter.
The amputation definition is the one that triggers the hadd punishment.
2
Extract the conditions for amputation
From the author's statement, list the conditions: mukallaf, secrecy, nisab of ten dirhams, hirz (guarded place or guardian), and the act of taking.
These conditions are cumulative — all must be present for amputation.
3
Analyze the condition of mukallaf
Understand who is excluded: minors and the insane are not liable for hadd, even if they steal. They are liable for financial restitution only.
If a group includes a non-mukallaf, amputation is averted from all due to doubt.
4
Examine secrecy (khafiyya)
Secrecy is required at the time of entering the hirz. For day thefts in a city, secrecy also required at departure. For night thefts, secrecy at departure is not required.
Open taking by force (robbery) or snatching does not count as theft for amputation.
5
Understand nisab and hirz
The minimum value is ten minted dirhams (about 30.6 grams silver). The property must be in a guarded place (hirz) — a locked room, or a guardian appointed.
The nisab and hirz conditions ensure that the punishment applies only to serious, premeditated theft.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
و أخذ مكلف خفية قدر عشرة دراهم مضروبة محرزة بمكان أو حافظ فيقطع
هو
كتاب السرقة
لما كانت صيانة الأموال مؤخرة عن صيانة النفوس والعقول والأعراض أخر زاجر ضياعها وهي في اللغة أخذ الشيء في خفاء وحيلة. يقال سرق منه مالاً وسرقه سرقاً وسرقة، ويسمى الشيء المسروق سرقة مجازاً؛ كذا في المغرب وأما في الشريعة فلها تعریفان : تعريف باعتبار الحرمة وتعريف باعتبار ترتب حكم شرعي وهو القطع. أما الأول فهو أخذ الشيء من الغير على وجه الخفية بغير حق، سواء كان نصاباً أو لا. وأما الثاني فهو ما ذكره المصنف بقوله : (هو أخذ مكلف خفية قدر عشرة دراهم مضروبة محرزة بمكان أو حافظ) أطلق في الأخذ فشمل الحقيقي والحكمي؛ فالأول هو أن يتولى السارق أخذ المتاع بنفسه، والثاني هو أن يدخل جماعة من اللصوص منزل رجل ويأخذوا متاعه ويحملوه على ظهر رجل واحد ويخرجوه من المنزل، فإن الكل يقطعون استحساناً وسيأتي، فخرج بالتكليف الصبي والمجنون لأن القطع عقوبة وهما ليسا من أهلها فهما مخصوصان من آية السرقة لكنهما يضمنان المال. وإن كان يجن [؟] وفيق فإن سرق في حال جنونه لم يقطع، وإن كان في حال الإفاقة قطع . ولو سرق جماعة فيهم صبي أو مجنون يدرأ عنهم القطع ؛ كذا في البدائع . وشمل الذكر والأنثى والحر والعبد ولو آبقاً والمسلم والكافر كما في البدائع. وخرج بقيد الخفية ما أخذ جهراً مغالبة أو نهباً أو اختلاساً فإنه لا قطع فيه. وأفاد بقوله «الأخذ خفية» إلى أن الشرط الخفية وقت الأخذ أو دخول الحرز ليلاً كان أو نهاراً، وأما الخفية في الانتهاء فإن كانت السرقة نهاراً في المصر فهي شرط أيضاً وما بين العشاء والعتمة من النهار ولذا قال في
الاختيار ولو دخل بين العشاء والناس منتشرون فهو بمنزلة النهار، وإن كانت السرقة ليلاً فليست بشرط حتى لو دخل البيت ليلاً خفية ثم أخذ المال مجاهرة ولو بعد مقاتلة من في يده
كتاب السرقة
قوله : ( وخرج باشتراط النصاب الخ) قال في النهر آخر الفصل الآتي : ولو أخرج نصاباً من
Book of Theft
And the taking of a legally responsible person secretly of the amount of ten minted dirhams from a guarded place or guardian, then he is amputated.
It is the Book of Theft
Since the protection of wealth is secondary to the protection of lives, intellects, and honors, the deterrent for its loss was placed last. It is in language: taking something secretly and by trickery. It is said: he stole money from him, and he stole it, a theft, and the stolen item is called theft metaphorically; thus in al-Mughrib. As for in Sharia, it has two definitions: a definition based on prohibition, and a definition based on the attachment of a legal ruling, which is amputation. The first is: taking something from another in a secret manner without right, whether it reaches the nisab or not. The second is: what the author mentioned in his statement: (It is the taking of a legally responsible person secretly of the amount of ten minted dirhams from a guarded place or guardian). He made 'taking' absolute, so it includes real and constructive taking. The first is that the thief personally takes the goods, and the second is that a group of thieves enter a man's home, take his goods, load them onto the back of a single man, and take them out of the house; then all are amputated by istihsan, as will come. So by 'legally responsible' the child and the insane person are excluded because amputation is a punishment and they are not subject to it, so they are excepted from the verse of theft, but they guarantee the property. And if he is insane at times, then if he steals during his insanity he is not amputated, and if during sanity he is amputated. And if a group steals, including a child or an insane person, amputation is averted from all; thus in al-Bada'i. And it includes male and female, free and slave, even if a runaway, and Muslim and disbeliever, as in al-Bada'i. And by the condition of secrecy, what is taken openly by overwhelming force, plunder, or snatching is excluded, for there is no amputation in it. And his statement 'taking secretly' indicates that the condition is secrecy at the time of taking or entering the guarded place, whether night or day. As for secrecy at the completion, if the theft is during the day in the city, it is also a condition, and what is between the evening and 'ishaa is considered day, hence he said in al-Ikhtiyar: And if he enters between 'ishaa and when people are dispersed, it is like daytime. And if the theft is at night, it is not a condition, so that if he enters the house secretly at night and then takes the money openly, even after fighting with the owner...
Book of Theft
His statement: (And by the condition of the nisab...) he said in al-Nahr at the end of the next chapter: And if he takes out a nisab from...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Theft (Kitab al-Sariqa) from Al-Bahr al-Raiq. The author begins by justifying the order of topics: wealth is protected after life, reason, and honor. He then provides the linguistic and legal definitions of theft. The legal definition for the purpose of amputation (hadd) is: the taking by a legally responsible person (mukallaf) of a minimum of ten dirhams in value from a guarded place (hirz) secretly. The commentary explains that 'taking' includes actual physical taking (haqiqi) and constructive taking (hukmi), such as when a group uses one person to carry the goods out. The condition of 'mukallaf' excludes minors and the insane; they are not amputated but must return or guarantee the property. If a person is intermittently insane, amputation applies only if theft occurs during sanity. If a group includes a minor or insane person, amputation is averted from all by way of dar' (avoidance of punishment in cases of doubt). The condition of secrecy excludes open taking (like robbery, plunder, snatching). Secrecy is required at the time of entering the hirz, but during the day in a city, secrecy during departure is also required. At night, secrecy at departure is not required. The passage ends by beginning a discussion on the condition of nisab, which is cut off.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqa): Legally: the taking of property secretly by a mukallaf from a guarded place, of a value of ten dirhams, leading to amputation.
حرز (hirz): A place or manner of guarding property, such as a locked room, or a person appointed as guardian.
مكلف (mukallaf): A legally responsible person: a Muslim who has reached puberty and is sane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition regarding secrecy for theft at night?
A) Secrecy is required both at the time of entering the hirz and at the time of taking the property.
B) Secrecy is not required at all for theft at night.
C) Secrecy is required at the time of entering the hirz, but not required at the time of taking the property if the theft occurs at night.
D) Secrecy is required only if the theft occurs during the day.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines theft for hadd amputation with five precise conditions: the thief must be mukallaf, the taking must be secret, the property must be of nisab (ten dirhams) and in a guarded place (hirz), and the taking must be either actual or constructive.

The Definition of Theft (Sariqa) and Conditions for Hadd
Defining sariqa linguistically and legally, listing conditions for hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Sharia definitions of sariqa and list the five conditions required for the hadd of amputation.

Core Idea
Hadd amputation requires a mukallaf, secret taking of 10 minted dirhams from guarded property.

The passage opens the Book of Theft by defining its subject. Theft (sariqa) has both linguistic and Sharia meanings. The Sharia definition relevant to punishment focuses on the act that triggers the fixed penalty (hadd) of hand-cutting. By understanding this definition, you will identify the precise conditions a theft must meet before the severe penalty can be applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition
From the text, note that linguistically theft means taking something secretly and cunningly. This is the common meaning before Sharia qualifies it.
The linguistic definition lacks conditions like nisab or guarding.
2
Distinguish the two Sharia definitions
The text mentions two Sharia definitions: one based on prohibition (any secret taking without right, regardless of value) and one based on the legal ruling (the specific act that leads to cutting). Focus on the second as it is the author's subject.
The second definition is the one mentioned by the author: 'taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian'.
3
Extract the conditions from the definition
Break down the author's definition into its components: (1) the taker must be mukallaf (accountable), (2) the taking must be secret (khafiyya), (3) the property must be of a minimum value (nisab: 10 dirhams), (4) the dirhams must be minted (maḍrūba), (5) the property must be guarded (muḥraza).
Each condition is essential; missing any one prevents the hadd.
4
Explain the condition of mukallaf
Mukallaf means the thief must be an adult and sane. Minors and insane persons are excluded from the hadd because punishment requires accountability. However, they remain civilly liable for the stolen property.
The text also notes that if a sane person later becomes insane, the theft during insanity does not incur cutting.
5
Recognize the scope of application
The condition applies to all persons regardless of gender, free or slave, Muslim or non-Muslim, as stated in al-Badā’i‘. The hadd is not limited by status.
This is a distinct Hanafi position; some earlier rulings had exceptions for slaves.

إلى أن الشرط الخفية وقت الأخذ أو دخول الحرز ليلاً كان أو نهاراً، وأما الخفية في الانتهاء فإن كانت السرقة نهاراً في المصر فهي شرط أيضاً وما بين العشاء والعتمة من النهار ولذا قال في الاختيار ولو دخل بين And the taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of the value of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian, so he is to be cut off. This is the book of theft. Since the protection of wealth is placed after the protection of lives, intellects, and honor, the deterrent against its loss is postponed. It is defined linguistically as taking a thing secretly and cunningly. It is said: he stole from him property, and he stole it, stealing and theft; and the stolen thing is metaphorically called theft; as in al-Mughrib. As for the Sharia, it has two definitions: a definition in terms of prohibition, and a definition in terms of the consequent legal ruling, which is cutting. The first is: taking a thing from another secretly without right, whether it reaches the nisab or not. The second is what the author mentioned in his statement: (It is the taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of the value of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian). He made 'taking' absolute, including real and constructive; the first is when the thief himself takes the goods, the second is when a group of thieves enter a man's house and take his goods and load them onto one man's back and take them out of the house; then all are cut off by istihsan, as will come. By 'mukallaf' the minor and the insane are excluded because cutting is a punishment and they are not fit for it; they are exceptions from the verse of theft, but they are liable for the property. And if he becomes insane, then if he steals during his insanity he is not cut off, and if he steals during his sanity he is cut off. And if a group steals including a minor or an insane person, the cutting is averted from them; as in al-Badai'. And it includes male and female, free and slave, even if an absconding slave, Muslim and kafir, as in al-Badai'. And by the condition of secrecy, what is taken openly by force or plunder or snatching is excluded, for there is no cutting in that. And his saying 'taking secretly' indicates that secrecy is required at the time of taking or entering the guarded place, whether by night or day. As for secrecy at the completion, if the theft is by day in a city, it is also a condition; and the time between isha and nightfall is considered daytime, as stated in al-Ikhtiyar: if he enters between isha while people are scattered, it is like daytime. And if the theft is by night, it is not a condition; so if he enters the house secretly by night, then takes the property openly, even after fighting with the owner, he is cut off for the sufficiency of the first secrecy. And the author did not clarify whether the consideration is that it be secret in the opinion of the thief or the victim, so it is fourfold: if the thief knows that the owner knows of his entry and the owner also knows, then no cutting; if neither knows, he is cut off by agreement; if the owner knows of his entry and the thief does not know that he knows, then he is cut off, sufficing with its being secret in the thief's opinion; if the opposite, i.e. the thief thinks the owner knows of him but the owner does not know, in al-Tabyin there is no cutting because it is open. And in al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, and al-Dhakhira, he is cut off, sufficing with its being secret in the opinion of either of them.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Theft and provides a definition of theft (sariqa) with its conditions for the application of the hadd penalty of amputation. The author first notes that wealth is protected after life, intellect, and honor, hence the chapter on theft follows those. Linguistically, theft is taking secretly and cunningly. In Sharia, there are two definitions: one in terms of prohibition (taking secretly without right, regardless of nisab) and another in terms of the legal ruling (cutting), which is the one given by the author: 'the taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of the value of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian.' The commentary expands each condition. 'Taking' includes both actual taking by the thief and constructive taking where a group loads goods onto one person; all are cut off by istihsan. 'Mukallaf' excludes minors and the insane, as cutting is a punishment and they are not liable to punishments. However, they remain civilly liable for the property. If a person becomes insane, cutting is only applied if the theft occurred during sanity. The condition of 'secrecy' (khafiyya) excludes open taking such as by force, plunder, or snatching. Secrecy is required at the time of entry into the guarded place, whether day or night, but for daytime theft in a city, secrecy must continue until the act is complete. For nighttime theft, initial secrecy suffices even if the taking becomes open later. A key point is whose perception of secrecy is considered: the thief's or the victim's? The text presents four scenarios. If both know, no cutting; if neither knows, cutting applies; if only the owner knows but the thief thinks it's secret, cutting applies based on the thief's perception; if the thief thinks the owner knows but the owner does not, there is a difference: al-Tabyin says no cutting because it is open, but other sources say cutting applies since it was secret in the opinion of either. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that cutting is applied if the thief believes it is secret, even if the owner knows, as stated in al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, and al-Dhakhira.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqa): Legally, taking property of a certain value (nisab) secretly from a guarded place without right, by a mukallaf, which triggers the hadd of amputation.
خفية (khafiyya): Secrecy: the taking must be hidden from the owner's or thief's perception; exact conditions vary by time and place.
مكلف (mukallaf): A person legally responsible (adult, sane); excludes minors and the insane from the hadd penalty.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum threshold value of stolen property (10 dirhams minted or equivalent) required for the hadd penalty.
حرز (hirz): A guarded place or guardian that protects property; theft from a hirz is a condition for hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The stolen property must be guarded (muḥraz).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hadd of hand-cutting for theft applies only when a mukallaf takes, secretly, property worth 10 minted dirhams from a guarded place.

The Condition of Secrecy (Khafiyya) and Its Legal Scenarios
Detailing the condition of khafiyya and its four scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of secrecy to determine whether the hadd applies in various scenarios, including the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Secrecy for theft is judged by the thief's belief; even open taking after secret entry at night suffices.

The condition of secrecy (khafiyya) is critical for the hadd penalty, but its precise meaning is not always self-evident. This lesson unpacks the text's detailed analysis of secrecy: when it must occur, how time of day affects it, and whose perception matters. We will also explore an unresolved ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school on whether the thief's belief alone suffices.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic requirement of secrecy
The text states that secrecy is required at the time of taking or entering the guarded place. Acts done openly (by force, plunder, snatching) do not qualify.
Secrecy is about the method, not the location per se.
2
Distinguish day and night regarding secrecy at completion
For daytime theft in a city, secrecy must continue until the act is complete; for nighttime theft, the initial secrecy at entry suffices even if the actual taking is open.
The time between isha and nightfall is treated as daytime.
3
Analyze the four scenarios of knowledge
The text presents four possibilities based on whether the thief and owner know of the theft. Create a 2x2 matrix: Thief knows owner knows / Thief does not know owner knows / Owner knows / Owner does not know.
Map each scenario to the ruling (cutting or no cutting) based on the majority view.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The source notes a disagreement: al-Tabyin holds that if the thief thinks the owner knows but the owner does not, there is no cutting because it is considered open. Other sources (al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, al-Dhakhira) hold that cutting applies because the act was secret in the opinion of either party.
The commentator implicitly prefers the latter view as the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
5
Apply the rule to practical cases
Given a scenario, determine whether the hadd applies by first checking the time of entry, then the perception of secrecy. Use the mufta bihi view (thief's belief suffices) for the ikhtilaf.
Remember: the hadd is averted only if both parties know, or if the thief believes the owner knows (in the minority view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الغير على وجه الخفية بغير حق، سواء كان نصاباً أو لا. وأما الثاني فهو ما ذكره المصنف بقوله : (هو أخذ مكلف خفية قدر عشرة دراهم مضروبة محرزة بمكان أو حافظ) أطلق في الأخذ فشمل الحقيقي والحكمي؛ فالأول هو لأن القطع عقوبة وهما ليسا من أهلها فهما مخصوصان من آية السرقة لكنهما يضمنان المال. وإن كان يجن، وفق فإن سرق في حال جنونه لم يقطع، وإن كان في حال الإفاقة قطع . ولو سرق جماعة فيهم صبي أو مجنون يدرأ عنهم القطع؛ كذا في البدائع . وشمل الذكر والأنثى والحر والعبد ولو آبقاً والمسلم والكافر كما في البدائع. وخرج بقيد الخفية ما أخذ جهراً مغالبة أو نهباً أو اختلاساً فإنه لا قطع فيه. وأفاد بقوله «الأخذ خفية» إلى أن الشرط الخفية وقت الأخذ أو دخول الحرز ليلاً كان أو نهاراً، وأما الخفية في الانتهاء فإن كانت السرقة نهاراً في المصر فهي شرط أيضاً وما بين العشاء والعتمة من النهار ولذا قال في الاختيار ولو دخل بين العشاء والناس منتشرون فهو بمنزلة النهار، وإن كانت السرقة ليلاً فليست بشرط حتى لو دخل البيت ليلاً خفية ثم أخذ المال مجاهرة ولو بعد مقاتلة من في يده قطع به للاكتفاء بالخفية الأولى. ولم يبين المصنف أن المعتبر كونها خفية على زعم السارق أو المسروق منه فهي رباعية، فلو كان السارق يعلم أن صاحب الدار يعلم بدخوله وعلم به صاحب الدار أيضاً فلا قطع أو لم يعلما فيقطع اتفاقاً، أو كان صاحب الدار يعلم بدخوله والسارق لا يعلم أنه يعلم فإنه يقطع اكتفاء بكونها خفية في زعم السارق، وإن كان على عكسه بأن زعم اللص بأن صاحب الدار علم به وصاحب الدار لم يعلم ففي التبيين لا يقطع لأنه جهر. وفي الخلاصة والمحيط والذخيرة أنه يقطع اكتفاء بكونها خفية في زعم أيهما كان
And the taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of the value of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian, so he is to be cut off. This is the book of theft. Since the protection of wealth is placed after the protection of lives, intellects, and honor, the deterrent against its loss is postponed. It is defined linguistically as taking a thing secretly and cunningly. It is said: he stole from him property, and he stole it, stealing and theft; and the stolen thing is metaphorically called theft; as in al-Mughrib. As for the Sharia, it has two definitions: a definition in terms of prohibition, and a definition in terms of the consequent legal ruling, which is cutting. The first is: taking a thing from another secretly without right, whether it reaches the nisab or not. The second is what the author mentioned in his statement: (It is the taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of the value of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian). He made 'taking' absolute, including real and constructive; the first is when the thief himself takes the goods, the second is when a group of thieves enter a man's house and take his goods and load them onto one man's back and take them out of the house; then all are cut off by istihsan, as will come. By 'mukallaf' the minor and the insane are excluded because cutting is a punishment and they are not fit for it; they are exceptions from the verse of theft, but they are liable for the property. And if he becomes insane, then if he steals during his insanity he is not cut off, and if he steals during his sanity he is cut off. And if a group steals including a minor or an insane person, the cutting is averted from them; as in al-Badai'. And it includes male and female, free and slave, even if an absconding slave, Muslim and kafir, as in al-Badai'. And by the condition of secrecy, what is taken openly by force or plunder or snatching is excluded, for there is no cutting in that. And his saying 'taking secretly' indicates that secrecy is required at the time of taking or entering the guarded place, whether by night or day. As for secrecy at the completion, if the theft is by day in a city, it is also a condition; and the time between isha and nightfall is considered daytime, as stated in al-Ikhtiyar: if he enters between isha while people are scattered, it is like daytime. And if the theft is by night, it is not a condition; so if he enters the house secretly by night, then takes the property openly, even after fighting with the owner, he is cut off for the sufficiency of the first secrecy. And the author did not clarify whether the consideration is that it be secret in the opinion of the thief or the victim, so it is fourfold: if the thief knows that the owner knows of his entry and the owner also knows, then no cutting; if neither knows, he is cut off by agreement; if the owner knows of his entry and the thief does not know that he knows, then he is cut off, sufficing with its being secret in the thief's opinion; if the opposite, i.e. the thief thinks the owner knows of him but the owner does not know, in al-Tabyin there is no cutting because it is open. And in al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, and al-Dhakhira, he is cut off, sufficing with its being secret in the opinion of either of them.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Theft and provides a definition of theft (sariqa) with its conditions for the application of the hadd penalty of amputation. The author first notes that wealth is protected after life, intellect, and honor, hence the chapter on theft follows those. Linguistically, theft is taking secretly and cunningly. In Sharia, there are two definitions: one in terms of prohibition (taking secretly without right, regardless of nisab) and another in terms of the legal ruling (cutting), which is the one given by the author: 'the taking by a mukallaf, secretly, of the value of ten minted dirhams, guarded in a place or by a guardian.' The commentary expands each condition. 'Taking' includes both actual taking by the thief and constructive taking where a group loads goods onto one person; all are cut off by istihsan. 'Mukallaf' excludes minors and the insane, as cutting is a punishment and they are not liable to punishments. However, they remain civilly liable for the property. If a person becomes insane, cutting is only applied if the theft occurred during sanity. The condition of 'secrecy' (khafiyya) excludes open taking such as by force, plunder, or snatching. Secrecy is required at the time of entry into the guarded place, whether day or night, but for daytime theft in a city, secrecy must continue until the act is complete. For nighttime theft, initial secrecy suffices even if the taking becomes open later. A key point is whose perception of secrecy is considered: the thief's or the victim's? The text presents four scenarios. If both know, no cutting; if neither knows, cutting applies; if only the owner knows but the thief thinks it's secret, cutting applies based on the thief's perception; if the thief thinks the owner knows but the owner does not, there is a difference: al-Tabyin says no cutting because it is open, but other sources say cutting applies since it was secret in the opinion of either. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that cutting is applied if the thief believes it is secret, even if the owner knows, as stated in al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, and al-Dhakhira.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqa): Legally, taking property of a certain value (nisab) secretly from a guarded place without right, by a mukallaf, which triggers the hadd of amputation.
خفية (khafiyya): Secrecy: the taking must be hidden from the owner's or thief's perception; exact conditions vary by time and place.
جهر (jahr): Open taking, as in force, plunder, or snatching; these do not meet the condition of secrecy.
رباعي (rubā'ī): Fourfold; referring to the four scenarios of knowledge between thief and owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a thief enters a house secretly at night, takes property openly, and the owner was aware but the thief thought the owner was asleep?
A) No cutting, because the taking was open.
B) No cutting, because the owner knew.
C) Cutting, because the thief believed it was secret.
D) Cutting, only if the owner was actually unaware.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies the hadd if the thief believes his act is secret, even if the owner knows; for night theft, only the initial entry need be secret.

Nisab for Hadd: Minted Dirhams, Valuation, and Multiple Thieves
Detailed expansion of nisab conditions and their applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for the nisab of cutting, apply valuation rules at time of theft and cutting, and identify when multiple thieves or separate hirz affect the hadd.

Core Idea
Cutting is only wajib for theft of ten minted dirhams or their equivalent; doubt averts the hadd.

The hadd of cutting (qat' al-yad) is a severe penalty, so its conditions are strictly defined. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the nisab (minimum value) required for theft to incur cutting. We will examine the requirement that the stolen goods be minted coins or their value equivalent, how value is determined over time, and special cases involving multiple thieves and separate guarded places.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab and its required form
Note that the nisab is ten dirhams, and these must be minted (madruba). Uncoined bullion or goods worth ten dirhams do not trigger cutting unless they are minted or their value in minted currency reaches the nisab.
The exception is half a dinar if its value equals ten dirhams.
2
Understand value fluctuation and taqwim
The value is considered both at the time of theft and at the time of cutting. If the decrease is due to a defect in the item, cutting still applies; if due to a fall in market price, cutting is averted according to zahir al-riwaya.
Evaluation must be done by two just experts; a single valuer is insufficient.
3
Apply rules for multiple thieves and separate hirz
The nisab is considered per thief, not per victim. If two thieves together steal a nisab from one person, neither is cut. If one thief steals from multiple victims in separate rooms of one house, he is cut if total reaches nisab; but stealing from different houses does not combine.
Separate acts from the same hirz after the owner repairs the breach do not combine for nisab.
4
Work through the examples in the passage
The passage provides several concrete examples: theft of a garment worth ten dirhams later recovered in another country where it is worth eight dirhams – no cutting. Also, the case of a single thief stealing from multiple owners in one house is cut, but two thieves stealing from one owner are not cut.
These examples illustrate the application of the conditions.

Source Text
