Items Exempt from Hadd Cutting: The Trifling and Permissible
Lists items exempt from hadd cutting with reasons and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on items for which the hadd of cutting is not applied, and identify the exceptions based on custom and manufacturing.

Core Idea
Hadd cutting is not applied for trifling or inherently permissible items, but exceptions arise when items are valuable and customarily guarded.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq delineates the boundaries of the hadd punishment for theft. While stealing any item is sinful, the severe penalty of hand amputation is reserved for theft of items that are both valuable and properly guarded. The text lists common natural and low-value items that fall below this threshold, but also introduces important exceptions based on custom and added value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is stealing specific items (wood, grass, reeds, fish, birds, game, arsenic, red ochre, lime). The ruling is that the hadd of cutting the hand is not applied for these items.
2
Note the two-fold rationale
Two reasons: (1) The items are trifling (tāfih) in value, and (2) their guarding (hirz) is deficient. The proof is the hadith of Aisha that the hand was not cut for trifling things during the Prophet's time.
The underlying principle is that hadd is averted by doubt (shubha).
3
Examine the exceptions based on manufacturing
If the item undergoes valuable craftsmanship (e.g., wood turned into furniture), the cut applies. Similarly, if the item is customarily guarded (e.g., teak, ebony), the cut applies.
The commentary cites examples like Baghdad mats and manufactured wood.
4
Examine the exceptions based on custom (urf)
Some items (like arsenic, woad, henna) were debated. The later Hanafi position follows custom: if it is customarily guarded in shops, the cut applies. This is also applied to items like coal, glass, salt, etc.
In al-Zahiriyya, chickens were excepted from the bird category, requiring cutting.
5
Apply the rule to new cases
To determine if the hadd applies, assess: Is the item customarily considered valuable? Is it customarily guarded? If both conditions are met (and nisab threshold reached), the cut applies. Otherwise, it is averted.
The mufta bihi position relies on custom to define tāfih and hirz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يقطع بخشب وحشيش وقصب وسمك وطير وصيد وزرنيخ ومغرة ونورة لأنه لا قطع فيما يوجد تافهاً مباحاً في دار الإسلام لقول رضي الله عنها : كانت اليد لا تقطع في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الشيء التافه أي الحقير. وما يوجد جنسه مباحاً في الأصل بصورته غير مرغوب فيه حقير لقلة الرغبات فيه والطباع لا تضن به فقل ما يوجد آخذه على كره من المالك فلا حاجة إلى شرع الزاجر ولهذا لم يجب القطع بسرقة ما دون النصاب ولأن الحرز فيها ناقص ألا ترى أن الخشب يلقى على الأبواب وإنما يدخل في الدار للعمارة لا للإحراز والطير يطير والصيد يفر، وكذا الشركة التي كانت فيه وهي على تلك الصفة تورث الشبهة والحد يندريء بها. أطلق الخشب وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يحدث فيه صنعة متقومة، فإن كان معمولاً قطع فيه كما في شرح الطحاوي كما يقطع في الحصر البغدادية كما في غاية البيان ومقيد بما إذا لم تجر العادة بإحرازه، فإن كان مما يحرز كالساج والأبنوس فإنه يقطع فيه وأطلق السمك فشمل الطري والمالح، والطير فشمل الدجاج والبط والحمام ونظر بعضهم في الزرنيخ فقال : ينبغي يقطع به لأنه يحرز ويصان في دكاكين العطارين كسائر الأموال واختلف في الوسمة والحناء والوجه القطع لأنه جرت العادة بإحرازه في الدكاكين والمغرة - بفتح الغين ـ الطين الأحمر ويجوز إسكانها. وألحق في المجتبى بما ذكر الفحم والأشنان والزجاج والملح والخزف، واستثنى في الظهيرية من الطير الدجاج فأوجب القطع فيه .
He said: 'And he is not cut for wood, grass, reeds, fish, birds, game, arsenic, red ochre, and lime.' Because there is no cutting for something found to be trifling, permissible in Dar al-Islam, due to the saying of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The hand was not cut in the time of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, for a trifling thing,' meaning insignificant. And what is found to be permissible in origin, its form is undesirable and insignificant due to few desires for it, and natures do not covet it, so rarely is its taker found against the will of the owner. So there is no need for a legal deterrent. For this reason, cutting is not obligatory for stealing less than the nisab. And because the guarding of such items is deficient. Do you not see that wood is placed at doors, and it is only brought into the house for construction, not for safeguarding. Birds fly, game escapes. Likewise, the partnership that existed in it on that description creates doubt, and the hadd is averted by it. He gave wood unrestricted, but it is restricted to when no valuable craftsmanship has been applied to it. If it is manufactured, the cut applies as in Sharh al-Tahawi, just as it applies to Baghdad mats as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And restricted to when it is not customary to guard it. If it is something that is guarded, like teak and ebony, then the cut applies. And he gave fish unrestricted, so it includes fresh and dried. And birds includes chickens, ducks, and pigeons. Some considered arsenic and said it should be cut for it because it is guarded and preserved in perfumers' shops like other properties. There is a difference of opinion regarding woad and henna; the preferred view is cutting because it is customary to guard them in shops. Al-mughra (with fat-ha on ghayn) is red clay, and it is permissible to pronounce it with sukun. In al-Mujtaba, coal, saltwort, glass, salt, and pottery were added to what was mentioned. In al-Zahiriyya, an exception was made from birds for chickens, making cutting obligatory for them.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq listing items for which the hadd of cutting does not apply. The reason is two-fold: these items are trifling (tāfih) in value and their genus is permissible (mubāḥ) in Dar al-Islam, meaning they can be taken without ownership restrictions. The proof is the hadith of Aisha that the hand was not cut for trifling things during the Prophet's time. Furthermore, such items are not typically guarded (hirz), e.g., wood left at doors for construction, birds that fly away, etc. However, if these items undergo manufacturing that adds value (e.g., crafted wood, Baghdad mats), or if they are valuable and customarily guarded (e.g., teak, ebony, arsenic, perfumers' goods), then cutting does apply. There is disagreement about some items like arsenic, woad, henna, and chickens. The commentary summarizes that the default ruling is no cutting for these items, but with exceptions based on custom (urf) and added value.

Key Terms
التافه (al-tāfih): Something of insignificant value; the hand is not cut for it.
الحرز (al-hirz): Proper storage or guarding; theft from a non-hirz does not incur cutting.
الشبهة (al-shubha): Doubt that averts the hadd; here, the doubt arises from deficient guarding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following items would likely incur the hadd cutting according to the commentary?
A) A piece of firewood from a pile at the door.
B) A kilogram of raw rice from a shop.
C) A wild pigeon found in a forest.
D) A manufactured wooden box.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of cutting is averted when the stolen item is trifling or customarily unguarded, but custom and manufacturing can elevate items into the punishable category.

Ruling on Perishable Items and Musical Instruments: No Cutting for Theft
Explains no cutting for perishable items, unguarded produce, and musical instruments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for theft of perishable items and musical instruments, and explain the textual proofs and rationales for dropping the penalty.

Core Idea
No cutting for perishable items, unguarded produce, and non-property due to doubts (shubuhat).

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh on hudud: the conditions under which the hadd penalty (amputation) is not applied for theft. The author lists several categories of items—fresh fruits, milk, meat, unharvested crops, intoxicating drinks, and musical instruments—for which the hadd is dropped. The reasoning combines textual evidence from two hadiths and logical considerations of spoilage, guarding (hirz), and property status (māliyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempted items
List all categories mentioned: moist fruit (including on trees), milk, meat, unharvested crops, intoxicating drinks, and musical instruments (like tanbūr).
The list is comprehensive but not exhaustive; the underlying principle is key.
2
Extract the primary textual evidence
Two hadiths are cited: 'No cutting for fruit or palm-heart' and 'No cutting for food.' Identify the exact Arabic phrases and their apparent meaning.
The hadiths are ahad, giving zanni thubut and dalalah.
3
Understand the Hanafi interpretation (wajh al-dalalah)
Hanafis interpret 'food' as items that quickly spoil, not all foodstuffs. They limit the exemption to perishables, as consensus applies cutting to wheat and sugar.
The interpretation is based on the underlying cause (illah) of rapid spoilage.
4
Analyze additional rationales
Beyond the hadith, rationales include: lack of proper guarding (hirz) for items on trees or unharvested crops, and doubt in property status for intoxicating drinks (must be poured out) and musical instruments (not considered māl).
Each reason independently raises a shubha that prevents the hadd.
5
Note the scholarly difference (ikhtilaf)
Al-Asbijabi added a condition (perhaps that the item must be on the tree), and the Shafi‘i school may apply cutting for valued fruits if properly guarded. Recognize the legitimate difference.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفاكهة رطبة أو على شجر ولبن ولحم وزرع لم يحصد وأشربة وطنبور) لأنه لا قطع فيما يتسارع إليه الفساد لقوله عليه السلام) لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر والكثر الجمار. وقال عليه السلام لا قطع في الطعام والمراد والله أعلم ما يتسارع إليه الفساد كالمهيأ للأكل منه وما في معناه كاللحم والتمر لأنه يقطع في الحنطة والسكر إجماعاً ولا إحراز فيما على الشجر وفي زرع لم يحصد ولتأول السارق في الأشربة المطربة الإراقة وبعضها ليس بمال، وفي مالية بعضها اختلاف فيتحقق شبهة عدم المال والطنبور من المعازف . أطلق في الفاكهة فشمل العنب والرطب على المختار لأنه يخاف الفساد من وجه وذكر الأسبيجابي أنه لا بد أن
He said: 'And for moist fruit or on a tree, milk, meat, unharvested crop, intoxicating drinks, and tambour.' Because there is no cutting for things that quickly spoil, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'No cutting for fruit or palm-heart' and al-kuthr is palm-heart. And he said, peace be upon him: 'No cutting for food,' and the meaning, Allah knows best, is what quickly spoils, like food prepared for eating and what is in its meaning like meat and dates. Because cutting does apply to wheat and sugar by consensus. And there is no guarding for what is on trees and unharvested crops, and due to the thief's interpretation that intoxicating drinks are to be poured out, and some are not property, and there is disagreement on the property value of some, so there is doubt of property. And tambour is from musical instruments. He gave fruit unrestricted, so it includes grapes and moist dates according to the chosen view, because corruption is feared from one aspect, and al-Asbijabi mentioned that it is necessary that ...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents another ruling from the matn: no cutting for items that are prone to quick spoilage, such as fresh fruits (even on trees), milk, meat, unharvested crops, intoxicating drinks, and musical instruments like the tambour (tanbūr). The proof is two hadiths: 'No cutting for fruit or palm-heart' and 'No cutting for food.' The Hanafi interpretation is that these hadiths apply to items that spoil quickly, not to staple grains like wheat and sugar, which do incur cutting by consensus. Additional reasons: such items are not properly guarded (e.g., fruit on tree), intoxicating drinks are not considered valid property (they must be poured out), and musical instruments are not considered property (māl) in the Hanafi school. The commentary notes that although fruits include grapes and fresh dates, some scholars like al-Asbijabi have further conditions.

Key Terms
الطنبور (al-tanbūr): A musical instrument; considered not to be property (mal) and therefore no cutting for its theft.
لا قطع (lā qat'a): No cutting (amputation) of the hand; the hadd penalty is dropped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school drops the hadd for theft of items that are perishable, unguarded, or not considered property, based on hadith and the principle of shubuhat.

No Cutting for Perishable Items
Ruling that hand is not cut for perishable items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hadd for perishable items and identify the proof (dalil) and the reasoning (shubha) that averts the cutting of the hand.

Core Idea
The hand is not cut for stealing items that quickly spoil because they are not properly guarded and the thief may have a doubt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi exception to the hadd of cutting for perishable items. The author cites prophetic hadith and elaborates on the rationale based on rapid spoilage, lack of hirz, and the operation of shubha. Understanding this ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle of averting hadd in cases of doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed exempt items
Read the matn's list: fresh fruit (on tree), milk, meat, unharvested crops, drinks, and tanbur. Note that these are items that quickly spoil or are not considered preserved property.
The list is not exhaustive; later commentary expands on each category.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the two hadith quotes: 'لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر' and 'لا قطع في الطعام'. Understand that the Prophet (saw) explicitly exempted these items from the hadd.
These ahadith are zanni in transmission and meaning, allowing for istihsan.
3
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The commentary explains that perishable items lack proper hirz (guarding) when on trees or unharvested, and the thief may have a doubt (shubha) about the property status, e.g., intoxicating drinks or musical instruments considered non-property.
The principle: 'al-hadd yudra' bi al-shubahat' (hadd is averted by doubts).
4
Note the exceptions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Observe that wheat and sugar are cut by consensus (except in famine). Also note Abu Hanifa's view on vinegar, which creates a shubha preventing a clear consensus.
The famine exception is based on necessity (darurah) as per hadith and Umar's practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفاكهة رطبة أو على شجر ولبن ولحم وزرع لم يحصد وأشربة وطنبور) لأنه لا قطع فيما يتسارع إليه الفساد لقوله عليه السلام) لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر والكثر الجمار. وقال عليه السلام لا قطع في الطعام والمراد والله أعلم ما يتسارع إليه الفساد كالمهيأ للأكل منه وما في معناه كاللحم والتمر لأنه يقطع في الحنطة والسكر إجماعاً ولا إحراز فيما على الشجر وفي زرع لم يحصد ولتأول السارق في الأشربة المطربة الإراقة وبعضها ليس بمال، وفي مالية بعضها اختلاف فيتحقق شبهة عدم المال والطنبور من المعازف . أطلق في الفاكهة فشمل العنب والرطب على المختار لأنه يخاف الفساد من وجه وذكر الأسبيجابي أنه لا بد أن يكون المسروق يبقى من حول إلى حول فإذا سرق شيء لا يبقى من حول إلى حول لا يجب القطع اهـ. وقيد بالرطبة لأنه يقع في اليابسة ويقطع في الزبيب والتمر. وأطلق في اللحم فشمل القديد منه لأنه يتوهم فيه الفساد وقيد بالأشربة لأنه يقطع في العسل والخل إجماعاً؛ كذا في التبيين. وفيه نظر لما نقله الناطفي عن المجرد قال أبو حنيفة : لا قطع في الخل لأنه قد صار خمراً مرة .اهـ. فلا يدعي الإجماع. وأطلق في الأشربة فشمل الحلو والمر وما إذا كان السارق مسلماً أو ذمياً. وأشار بالطنبور إلى جميع آلات اللهو. وفي الظهيرية وغيرها: والقطع في الحنطة وغيرها إجماعاً إنما هو في غير سنة القحط أما فيها فلا، سواء كان مما يتسارع الفساد إليه أو لا، لأنه عن ضرورة ظاهرة أو هي تبيح التناول وعنه عليه السلام «لا قطع في مجاعة مضطرة وعن عمر رضي الله عنه : لا قطع في عام سنة
His saying: (And fresh fruit or on a tree, milk, meat, unharvested crops, drinks, and tanbur) because there is no cutting for what quickly perishes due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No cutting for fruit or the core (kathar), and kathar is the spadix.' And he said (peace be upon him): 'No cutting for food.' The intended meaning, Allah knows best, is what quickly perishes, like prepared food and what is in its meaning like meat and dates. Because cutting applies to wheat and sugar by consensus. And there is no guarding (hirz) for what is on the tree or unharvested crops, and due to the thief's interpretation regarding intoxicating drinks that they are to be poured away, and some of them are not property, and there is disagreement about some being property, so doubt of non-property is realized. The tanbur is among musical instruments. He made fruit absolute, including grapes and fresh dates according to the chosen view because spoilage is feared in one respect. Al-Isbijabi mentioned that the stolen item must remain from year to year; if something is stolen that does not remain from year to year, cutting is not obligated. He restricted to fresh because dry is included, and cutting applies to raisins and dates. He made meat absolute, including dried meat because spoilage is suspected. He restricted drinks because cutting applies to honey and vinegar by consensus, as in al-Tibyan. But there is consideration due to what al-Natafi narrated from al-Mujarrad: Abu Hanifa said: No cutting in vinegar because it has become bitter wine. So consensus cannot be claimed. He made drinks absolute, including sweet and bitter, and whether the thief is Muslim or dhimmi. By tanbur he intended all instruments of amusement. In al-Zahiriyyah and others: Cutting for wheat and others by consensus applies only in non-famine years; as for famine years, no, whether it perishes quickly or not, because of necessity or because it permits consumption. And from him (peace be upon him): 'No cutting in a forced famine.' And from Umar (ra): No cutting in a year of drought.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's list of items for which the hand is not cut due to rapid spoilage. The matn states: 'fresh fruit or on a tree, milk, meat, unharvested crops, drinks, and tanbur.' The proof is the hadith 'la qat' fi thamar wa la kathar' and 'la qat' fi al-ta'am' — where 'ta'am' is interpreted as perishable food. The reason is that such items are not normally guarded (hirz) when on trees or unharvested, and the thief may have an excuse (like intending to dispose of intoxicants). Additionally, some of these items (like tanbur, a musical instrument) are not legitimate property. The commentary refines: fruit includes fresh grapes and dates (since dry ones are valuable and cutting applies for raisins and dates). Meat includes dried meat. Drinks include all liquids except those that become vinegar (Abu Hanifa exempts vinegar due to its transformation). The ruling is also conditional on non-famine years; in famine, no cutting applies due to necessity. This reflects the Hanafi principle of averting hadd by doubts (shubhat).

Key Terms
يتسارع إليه الفساد (yatasāra' ilayhi al-fasād): Quickly perishing, spoiling within a short time.
ثمر (thamr): Fruit, especially fresh fruit on the tree.
كثر (kathar): The core of a palm spadix (spadix).
حرز (hirz): Proper guarding or secure storage of property.
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following items would still incur hadd when stolen in a normal (non-famine) year?
A) Fresh grapes
B) Wheat
C) Milk
D) Ripe dates

Key Takeaway
The hand is not cut for stealing items that quickly perish, due to prophetic hadith and the Hanafi principles of averting hadd by doubts and lack of proper guarding.

Exceptions to Hadd for Theft: Mushaf, Mosque Door, Cross
Listing exemptions from hadd with rationales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of objects exempt from the hadd penalty for theft and explain the Hanafi rationale (shubha, lack of hirz, or absence of owner) for each exception.

Core Idea
Hadd is averted by doubt: the thief's plausible religious intent or the object's public nature nullifies the penalty.

The Hanafi school carefully defines when the penalty for theft (cutting the hand) applies. Certain objects are protected by doubt (shubha) or lack of secure storage (hirz). This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq lists several such exceptions: the Mushaf (Quran), mosque doors, gold crosses, chess sets, and backgammon. Understanding these exceptions clarifies how the principle 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that no cutting (hadd) applies for stealing certain objects. List each object mentioned: Mushaf (even with gold/silver covers), mosque door, gold cross, chess, backgammon, notebooks (books), free child with ornaments, vessel containing wine.
Also implicitly: mats, lamps of mosque, Ka'ba drapes.
2
Categorise the rationale
For each object, identify the reason given: (a) shubha in the thief's intention (Mushaf, cross, chess, backgammon, vessel with wine, free child), (b) lack of hirz (mosque door), or (c) absence of a private owner (Ka'ba drapes).
Shubha can arise from plausible religious motive or from subsidiary value.
3
Analyse the proof
The Hanafi proof is qiyas: analogising these cases to established examples of doubt that avert hadd. The operative principle is that any doubt (shubha) prevents the penalty, based on the maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat'.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli) not textual (dalil naqli) in this passage.
4
Compare with other schools
Note that other madhahib may disagree on some cases. For example, Imam al-Shafi'i may consider a gold cross as valuable property if stolen by a Muslim, whereas the Hanafis give weight to the thief's potential claim of religious duty.
The thamarah al-khilaf: whether hadd is applied or not affects the punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومصحف ولو محلى) أي لا قطع في سرقة مصحف ولو كان عليه حلية من ذهب أو فضة لأن الآخذ يتأول في أخذه القراءة والنظر فيه ولأنه لا مالية له على اعتبار المكتوب وإحرازه لأجله لا للجلد والأوراق والحلية وإنما هي توابع ولا معتبر بالتبع كمن سرق آنية فيها خمر وقيمة الآنية تربو على النصاب، وكمن سرق صبياً حراً وعليه حلي. قال في المبسوط : ألا ترى أنه لو سرق ثوباً لا يساوي عشرة ووجد في جيبه عشرة مصرورة لم يعلم بها لم أقطعه، وإن كان يعلم بها فعليه القطع وقد قدمناه وسيأتي أنه لا قطع في الدفاتر وهي الكتب شرعية كانت أو لا قوله : (وباب مسجد) لعدم الإحراز فصار كباب الدار بل أولى لأنه يحرز بباب الدار ما فيها ولا يحرز بباب المسجد ما فيه حتى لا يجب القطع بسرقة متاعه . قال فخر الإسلام: فإن اعتاد سرقة أبواب المساجد فيجب أن يعزر ويبالغ فيه ويحبس حتى يتوب اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون كذلك سارق البزابيز من الميض وأشار إلى أنه لا قطع في سرقة حصره وقناديله وكذا استار الكعبة وإن كانت محرزة لعدم المالك قوله : وصليب ذهب وشطرنج ونرد) لأنه يتأول من أخذها الكسر نهياً عن المنكر بخلاف الدرهم الذي عليه التمثال لأنه ما أعد للعبادة فلا تثبت شبهة إباحة الكسر . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في حرز أو لا . والشطرنج بكسر الشين. وفي ضياء الحلوم : النرد الذي يلعب به وهو فارسي معرب وقل ما يأتلف النون والراء في كلمة واحدة إلا بدخل بينهما .اهـ. وسيأتي في الشهادات أنه كل لعب لا يحتاج لاعبه إلى فكر
His saying: (And the Mushaf, even if decorated) i.e., no cutting for stealing a Mushaf, even if it has gold or silver ornamentation, because the taker interprets his taking as for reading and looking, and because it has no monetary value in terms of its written content, and its preservation is for that purpose, not for the leather, pages, and ornamentation; they are merely subsidiary, and no consideration is given to subsidiary items, like the case of one who steals a vessel containing wine while the vessel's value exceeds the nisab, and like one who steals a free child with ornaments. He said in al-Mabsut: Do you not see that if someone stole a garment worth less than ten dirhams, and found in its pocket ten dirhams tied up without knowing, I would not cut his hand; but if he knew, cutting applies. And we have mentioned this earlier. And it will come that there is no cutting for notebooks, i.e., books, whether religious or not. His saying: (And the mosque door) due to lack of hirz, so it becomes like the door of a house, or even more so, because the house door guards what is inside, while the mosque door does not guard what is inside, so cutting is not obligated for stealing its contents. Fakhr al-Islam said: If someone habitually steals mosque doors, he should be punished with ta'zir, severely, and imprisoned until he repents. Similarly, the stealer of spouts from the ablution place should be treated likewise. And he indicated that there is no cutting for stealing its mats and lamps, likewise the drapes of the Ka'ba even if they are guarded, due to the absence of an owner. His saying: (And a gold cross, chess and backgammon) because the taker of these interprets his taking as breaking them as an injunction against evil, unlike a dirham with an image, because it is not made for worship, so no doubt of permissibility of breaking. He made it absolute, including whether it is in a guarded place or not. Chess (shatranj) with a kasra on the shin. In Diya' al-Huloom: Backgammon (nard) is that which is played with; it is a Persian word arabicized, and rarely do noon and ra come in one word except with an insertion. And it will come in the chapter of testimonies that it is all play that does not require the player to think.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage completes the list of items exempt from the hadd for theft. Three categories are covered: (1) The Mushaf (Quran) even with gold/silver covers: the thief may claim he took it for reading, and the value lies in the content, not the material; also it is subsidiary to the writing, so the hadd is averted by doubt (shubha). Analogous cases: a vessel containing wine, or a free child with ornaments. (2) The mosque door: because it does not serve as a secure place for the mosque's contents; however, habitual thieves of mosque doors receive ta'zir. Also items like mats, lamps, and Ka'ba drapes are exempt because there is no specific owner (they are public property). (3) Gold cross, chess set, backgammon: the thief plausibly intended to break them as a religious duty (enjoining good and forbidding evil), creating a doubt that averts the hadd. Unlike a coin with an image, which is not a religious object. Chess and backgammon are defined as games of chance. The commentary ends with a note that these cases will be discussed further in the book of testimonies.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity in the case that prevents application of the hadd penalty.
إحراز (hirz): Secure storage; a place where property is kept safely.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment administered by the judge for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hand not cut for stealing a gold cross according to this passage?
A) Because it is not considered property.
B) Because the thief can claim he intended to break it as an act of enjoining good.
C) Because a cross is too small to reach the nisab.
D) Because it is not guarded.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts the hadd penalty for theft when any plausible doubt (shubha) exists, such as a religious motive or lack of secure storage.

Why Stealing a Free Child with Jewelry Does Not Incur Amputation
Ruling statement with proof explaining absence of hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing a free child with jewelry and explain the two reasons (mal not present, ta'wil creating shubha) that repel the hadd.

Core Idea
A free child is not property; subsidiary jewelry does not trigger hadd, and a plausible excuse creates shubha.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'iq explains a crucial detail in the law of theft (hadd al-sariqa): stealing a free minor who is wearing jewelry does not lead to amputation. At first glance, one might think the jewelry itself could justify the penalty, but the Hanafi school provides two clear reasons. Let's walk through the legal reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act under discussion? Stealing a free child (sabi hurr) who has jewelry (huly) on him. The ruling: no amputation (la qat') for the thief.
The passage begins by stating the ruling: 'wa sabi hurr wa law ma'ahu huly' – even if he has jewelry.
2
Analyze the first reason – the child is not property
The primary reason: a free person is not considered property (mal) in Islamic law. Theft that triggers hadd requires stealing mal from a hirz. Since the child himself is not mal, the essential condition is absent.
This is a strong textual basis: 'li-anna al-hurr laysa bi-mal' – the free person is not property.
3
Analyze the second reason – subsidiary nature of jewelry
Even though the jewelry is property, it is subsidiary (tabi') to the child. In Hanafi law, a subsidiary object does not independently establish the hadd if the primary object is not subject to theft.
The text says 'ma 'alayhi min al-huli tabi' lahu' – what is on him of jewelry is subsidiary to him.
4
Consider the shubha (doubt) from possible excuse
The thief may have a valid interpretation (ta'wil): he might have taken the child to silence him or carry him to his wet nurse. This creates a legal doubt (shubha) that repels the hadd.
The text: 'li-annahu yata'awwal fi akhdh al-sabi iskatahu aw hamlahu ila murdi'atihi' – he may be interpreted as intending to quiet him or take him to the nurse.
5
Define key term and note generality
The term 'huly' (pl. of hala) is defined as ornaments of gold, silver, or gems. The ruling is general, covering any free child, including those who cannot walk or speak.
The text specifies that it includes the child who cannot walk or speak – emphasizing that even infants are covered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصبي حر ولو معه حلى لأن الحر ليس بمال وما عليه من الحلي تبع له ولأنه يتأول في أخذ الصبي إسكاته أو حمله إلى مرضعته أطلقه فشمل الصبي الذي لا يمشي ولا يتكلم. والحلي - بضم الحاء - جمع حَلى - بفتحها - ما يلبس من ذهب أو فضة أو جوهر.
His saying: (And a free child, even if he has jewelry, because the free person is not property, and what is on him of jewelry is subsidiary to him, and because the taker of the child may be interpreted as intending to silence him or carry him to his wet nurse. This is general, including a child who cannot walk or speak. Al-huly (with damma on ha) is plural of hala (with fatha), meaning what is worn of gold, silver, or gems.)
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn with commentary. It addresses the case of stealing a free minor child who is wearing jewelry. The ruling: no amputation (la qat') for the thief. Two reasons are given: (1) A free person, even a child, is not considered property (mal) in Islamic law. Since the child himself is not property, stealing him cannot fulfill the condition of stealing property to trigger the hadd. (2) The jewelry on the child is subsidiary (tabi') to the child; it is not the primary object of theft. Additionally, the thief may have a valid excuse (ta'wil), such as taking the child to quiet him or take him to his nurse, which creates a shubha (legal doubt) that repels the hadd. The ruling applies generally to any free child, including infants who cannot walk or speak. The term 'huly' is defined as ornaments of gold, silver, or gems.

Key Terms
صبي حر (sabi hurr): A free minor child, not a slave.
حلي (huly): Ornaments worn, made of gold, silver, or gems. Plural of hala.
تبع (tabi'): Subsidiary; the jewelry follows the child and is not the primary object of theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Stealing a free child with jewelry does not incur amputation because the child is not property, the jewelry is subsidiary, and a possible excuse creates shubha.

Ruling on Stealing a Large Slave and Ledgers
Distinguishes between large and small slaves and types of ledgers for hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between large and small slaves regarding amputation and explain the Hanafi dispute over ledgers.

Core Idea
Amputation for theft applies to large slaves but not small children; ledgers only trigger hadd if the content has no value.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two nuanced cases in the law of theft: stealing a slave and stealing ledgers. The key is whether the object qualifies as 'property' in the full sense. For slaves, the size (ability to express) matters; for ledgers, the purpose of the theft determines whether amputation applies. An intra-Hanafi dispute adds depth to the ruling on small slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage treats two distinct sub-rulings: (1) stealing a large versus a small slave, and (2) stealing ledgers versus account ledgers. Note the framework: for each, the hadd (amputation) may or may not apply.
2
Define large and small slave
The text defines 'large' (kabir) as a slave who can express himself, regardless of age; 'small' (saghir) as one who cannot. This classification affects the ruling: for a large slave, amputation applies because the act is considered usurpation or deception; for a small slave, there is a dispute.
The large category includes sleeping, insane, and blind slaves.
3
Analyze the ledger distinction
The general rule is that the content of ledgers is not property – the thief intends the paper. Account ledgers (دفاتر الحساب) are an exception because the content is not valuable, so the paper is the target, and amputation applies. For ledgers with beneficial content (e.g., hadith, tafsir), there is disagreement: some treat them like account ledgers, others like religious books (no cut).
Books of magic and philosophy are clear cases where amputation applies because no one values the content.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on small slaves
Abu Yusuf holds that amputation does not apply to a small slave because he is partly human and partly property – istihsan repels the hadd due to shubha. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, i.e., the Sahibayn) hold that the slave is absolute property because he is beneficial, so amputation applies. The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that amputation applies if the value reaches nisab.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): if not amputated, the punishment is ta'zir and compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وعبد كبير ودفاتر بخلاف الصغير ودفاتر الحساب لأنه في الكبير غصب أو خداع وهي متحققة في الصغير. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يقطع وإن كان صغيراً لا يعقل ولا يتكلم استحساناً لأنه آدمي من وجه مال من وجه ولهما أنه مال مطلق لكونه منتفعاً به أو منتفعاً يصير به إلا أنه انضم إليه معنى الآدمية، ولو كانت قيمته أقل من النصاب أذنه شيء يكمل النصاب يقطع باعتبار الضم أراد بالكبير المميز المعبر عن نفسه بالغاً كان أو صبياً، وبالصغير الذي لا يعبر عن نفسه. وأطلق في الكبير فشمل النائم والمجنون والأعمى. والمقصود من الدفاتر ما فيها وذلك ليس بمال إلا دفتر الحساب لأن ما فيه لا يقصد بالأخذ فكان المقصود هو الكاغد والمراد بالدفاتر صحائف فيها كتابة من عربية أو شعر أو حديث أو تفسير أو فقه مما هو من علم الشريعة، وقد اختلف في غيرها فقيل ملحقة بدفاتر الحساب فيقطع فيها، وقيل بكتب الشريعة لأن معرفتها قد تتوقف على اللغة والشعر والحاجة. وإن قلت: كفت في ايراث الشبهة ومقتضى هذا أن لا يختلف في القطع بسرقة كتب السحر والفلسفة لأنه لا يقصد ما فيها لأهل الديانة فكانت سرقة صرفاً. والمراد بدفاتر الحساب دفاتر أهل الديون وقولهم لأن المقصود الكاغد يدل على أن المراد به الذي مضى حسابه وقد قيل به كما ذكره الشمني . وأما الدفاتر التي في الديوان المعمول بها فالمقصود علم ما فيها فلا قطع وأما دفاتر مثل علم الحساب والهندسة فهو كغيره فلا قطع بسرقته لأنها ككتب الأدب والشعر. وقيد بالدفاتر لأنه لو سرق الورق والجلد قبل الكتابة قطع ذكره الشمني
His saying: (And a large slave and ledgers, unlike a small child and account ledgers, because in the case of the large slave there is usurpation or deception, and that is realized in the small child. Abu Yusuf said: Amputation is not applied even if the slave is small, incapable of reason or speech, by istihsan, because he is a human in one aspect and property in another. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that he is absolute property because he is beneficial or can be made beneficial, except that the meaning of humanity is attached to him. If his value is less than nisab, but something of his body completes the nisab, amputation is applied by aggregation. By 'large' is meant the discerning one who can express himself, whether adult or child; by 'small' is meant one who cannot express himself. The word 'large' is general, including the sleeping, insane, and blind. The intent of ledgers is their content, and that is not property except for account ledgers, because what is in them is not intended to be taken, so the intended object is the paper. Ledgers meant are sheets containing writing in Arabic, poetry, hadith, tafsir, or fiqh from the sciences of Shari'ah. There is a difference of opinion about other types: some say they are like account ledgers and amputation is applied; others say they are like books of Shari'ah, because knowledge may depend on language and poetry and need. If you say they suffice to create doubt, then it follows that there is no difference in amputation for stealing books of magic and philosophy, because their content is not intended by religious people, so it is pure theft. Account ledgers refer to ledgers of debtors. Their statement that the intended object is the paper indicates that it refers to what has already been accounted. It has been said as such, as mentioned by al-Shumni. As for the ledgers used in the diwan, the intent is the knowledge in them, so no amputation. As for ledgers of arithmetic and geometry, they are like other books, so no amputation because they are like books of literature and poetry. It is restricted to ledgers because if one steals the paper and leather before writing, amputation is applied, as mentioned by al-Shumni.)
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe covering two sub-rulings: (1) stealing a large slave versus a small child, and (2) stealing ledgers versus account ledgers. For the slave, the matn states that amputation applies to a large slave but not to a small child. The commentary explains: for a large slave, the thief's act is either usurpation (ghasb) or deception, while for a small child, the act is not theft in the full sense. Abu Yusuf's view is that even for a small slave, amputation is not applied by istihsan, because the slave is partly human and partly property. But the majority (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the text, actually the two are the Sahibayn) hold that a small slave is absolute property, so amputation applies. The text clarifies that 'large' means a slave who can express himself, while 'small' cannot. The large category includes sleeping, insane, and blind slaves. For ledgers, the general rule is that what is in the ledger is not property except for account ledgers (دفاتر الحساب) where only the paper is intended. For ledgers with beneficial content (hadith, tafsir, etc.), there is disagreement: some treat them like account ledgers and cut, others treat them like religious books and do not cut. The preferred view is that books of magic and philosophy are pure theft because their content is not valuable to religious people. Account ledgers specifically refer to debt ledgers where the accounts are already settled. The ledgers of the diwan (official records) are intended for their knowledge, so no amputation. Ledgers of arithmetic and geometry are like literature books. The key distinction is whether the primary object is the paper or the content. If the paper and leather are stolen before writing, amputation applies because the value is in the material.

Key Terms
عبد كبير (abd kabir): A slave who is discerning (mumayyiz) and can express himself, whether adult or child.
دفاتر الحساب (dafatir al-hisab): Account ledgers; the content is not intended, only the paper is valuable.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property wrongfully, but not secretly (like theft).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for which type of ledger does amputation definitely apply?
A) Ledger of poetry
B) Account ledger
C) Ledger of hadith
D) Ledger of geometry

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft applies to large slaves and account ledgers, but for small slaves and beneficial ledgers, the ruling depends on the value of the property and the thief's intent.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
8 min
Why Stealing a Dog Does Not Incur Hadd
Explains why amputation is not applied for stealing a dog or cheetah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on stealing a dog or cheetah and explain why the shubha from ikhtilaf repels the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
Scholarly disagreement on a dog's property status creates shubha, preventing the hadd penalty for theft.

In this lesson, we examine a specific exception within the law of theft (sariqa): why the amputation penalty is not applied when someone steals a dog or a cheetah. The passage provides two interlocking reasons: the animal's original permissibility (mubah al-asl) and the existence of scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) on its property status. Understanding this ruling clarifies how shubha (doubt) functions as a barrier to hudud punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
First, note that the ruling is about the act of stealing a dog or cheetah. The text states that no amputation (qat') is applied. Determine the precise hukm level: 'hadd not wajib' (i.e., the penalty is not incurred).
The ruling is a negative one: the hadd does not apply.
2
Extract the reasons (talil)
The text gives two reasons: (a) these animals are 'mubah al-asl' – originally permissible, not inherently owned property; (b) scholars have openly differed on the property status of dogs, which creates a shubha that repels the hadd. Identify how each reason independently contributes to the ruling.
The term 'shubha' is key – it indicates a valid doubt that blocks the punishment.
3
Understand the scope of the generality
The ruling applies generally: it includes a dog wearing a gold or silver collar, whether the thief knew about the collar or not. The collar is subsidiary (tabi') to the dog, like jewelry on a free child. This shows that the shubha from the dog's status overrides any value the collar might add.
The principle of 'tabi' (subsidiary) prevents the collar from being treated as independent property.
4
Contrast with other schools
Though the passage does not name the opposing school, the mention of ikhtilaf points to a real dispute. Most other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider dogs as property (mal) and would apply hadd for theft of a valuable dog. State the Hanafi position and the opposing view.
The Hanafi school relies on the shubha, while others see no such doubt.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a thief who steals a dog (even with a gold collar) does not have his hand cut off, but may still be liable for compensation (damages). Under Shafi'i law, if the dog's value reaches nisab, the hadd applies.
This shows that ikhtilaf can change the outcome significantly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكلب وفهد لأن من جنسها يوجد مباح الأصل غير مرغوب فيه ولأن واختلف العلماء ظاهر في مالية الكلب فأورث شبهة أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان عليه بین طوق ذهب أو فضة علم به أو لم يعلم لأنه تبع له كالصبي الحر إذا كان عليه حلي
His saying: And a dog and a cheetah, because their type is found as originally permissible (mubah al-asl), not desirable, and because scholars have openly differed on the property status of the dog, thus causing doubt (shubha). This is general, including if the dog has a collar of gold or silver, whether the thief knew or not, because it is subsidiary to him, like the free child when he has jewelry.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn stating that no amputation is applied for stealing a dog or a cheetah (fahd). The commentary provides two reasons: (1) Dogs and similar animals are originally permissible (mubah al-asl) – i.e., they are not inherently owned property; they are wild animals that can be taken freely. (2) Scholars have differed on whether dogs qualify as property capable of being stolen for hadd. This difference of opinion itself creates a shubha that repels the hadd punishment. The ruling applies generally, even if the dog wears a gold or silver collar, because the collar is subsidiary to the dog, similar to the case of a free child wearing jewelry. Thus, the shubha from the dog's uncertain property status overrides the value of the collar.

Key Terms
كلب (kalb): Dog
فهد (fahd): Cheetah or hunting leopard
مباح الأصل (mubah al-asl): Originally permissible to take; not owned by anyone inherently.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of a fixed punishment.
تبع (tabi'): Subsidiary; something that follows the ruling of its principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is amputation not applied when someone steals a dog?
A) Because a dog's value is less than nisab.
B) Because dogs are impure and cannot be owned.
C) Because of scholarly disagreement on the dog's property status, causing shubha.
D) Because dogs are not considered animals.

Key Takeaway
Scholarly disagreement on whether dogs are property creates a shubha that prevents the hadd penalty for theft, even if the dog wears a valuable collar.
